# Agent 5 — Quotient Scalar Readout / Lost-\(T\) Replacement

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
H_m^{\quot}(s)
=
\omega_h\!\left(
\pi_{\mathfrak f}\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m),
A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\right)
}
\]

is a coherent **quotient scalar**, and its odd coefficients are exactly the quotient determinants

\[
D_{2r+1}
=
u_{2r+1}v_5-u_5v_{2r+1}.
\]

It inherits the basic analytic machinery of \(H_m\): holomorphy, odd Cauchy sampling after taking the odd fixed-sector channel, finite-order quotient determinacy, and sampled-value reconstruction.

But it **does not currently replace** the old scalar pipeline

\[
H_m(s)=\Phi_K(\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m)).
\]

The missing theorem is still:

\[
\boxed{
H_m^{\quot}\equiv0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\text{no RH-relevant off-line anomaly.}
}
\]

At present, \(H_m^{\quot}\equiv0\) allows nonzero fixed-sector anomalies parallel to \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\). Those are exactly the discarded line-gauge / \(T\)-direction anomalies. The attached prompt identifies this as the Agent 5 kill-switch: decide whether \(H_m^{\quot}\) can replace \(H_m\), or whether quotient scalarization loses RH-relevant detection. 

---

# 1. Role of the old scalar \(H_m\)

The existing scalar is

\[
H_m(s)
=
\Phi_K\!\left(
\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m)
\right).
\]

Its role is not cosmetic. It performs the current proof’s **representative-level transform extraction**.

In the fixed-sector channel,

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}
=
u_{2r+1}I+v_{2r+1}S,
\]

and

\[
\Phi_K(I)=0,
\qquad
\Phi_K(S)=2.
\]

Therefore

\[
\Phi_K(A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f})
=
2v_{2r+1}.
\]

So \(H_m\) directly sees the \(S\)-coordinate \(v_{2r+1}\). In particular,

\[
\Phi_K(A_7^{\mathfrak f})=2v_7,
\qquad
T=\frac{v_7}{c}.
\]

Thus the old scalar pipeline detects precisely the \(T\)-visible hidden-state coordinate. That is why Bottleneck C became hard: the local obstruction is \(S\)-visible, hence \(T\)-visible.

The current analytic machinery around \(H_m\) uses:

\[
H_m \text{ holomorphic and odd},
\]

the microscopic expansion

\[
H_m(z/Q^2)
=
\sum_{r\ge0}
c_{2r+1}(m)\frac{z^{2r+1}}{Q^{2r+4}},
\]

and sampled Cauchy reconstruction

\[
\Xi_\zeta^{(N)}(m)
=
\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}H_m(s_j).
\]

A quotient scalar must reproduce this pipeline without using \(v_7,T,\Phi_K(A_7^{\mathfrak f})\) as independent data.

---

# 2. Definition of the quotient scalar

Define the alternating determinant form on the fixed sector

\[
\omega_h(uI+vS,\;u'I+v'S)
:=
uv'-u'v.
\]

Let

\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}=u_5I+v_5S.
\]

Define

\[
\boxed{
H_m^{\quot}(s)
:=
\omega_h\!\left(
\pi_{\mathfrak f}\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m),
A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\right).
}
\]

If

\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m)
=
\sum_{r\ge0}
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}(m)s^{2r+1}
+
\text{even/background terms},
\]

then the odd quotient scalar is

\[
\boxed{
H_{m,\odd}^{\quot}(s)
=
\frac12
\left(
H_m^{\quot}(s)-H_m^{\quot}(-s)
\right).
}
\]

For readability I write this odd part again as \(H_m^{\quot}\). This is the object relevant to the finite odd-channel package.

---

# 3. Coefficients

Write

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}
=
u_{2r+1}I+v_{2r+1}S.
\]

Then

\[
\omega_h(A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f})
=
u_{2r+1}v_5-u_5v_{2r+1}.
\]

Therefore

\[
\boxed{
H_m^{\quot}(s)
=
\sum_{r\ge0}
D_{2r+1}(m)s^{2r+1},
}
\]

where

\[
\boxed{
D_{2r+1}
=
u_{2r+1}v_5-u_5v_{2r+1}.
}
\]

In particular,

\[
D_5
=
u_5v_5-u_5v_5
=
0,
\]

and

\[
D_7
=
u_7v_5-u_5v_7
=
\Delta_7.
\]

So \(H_m^{\quot}\) is exactly the analytic scalar whose odd coefficients are the quotient determinant coefficients.

---

# 4. Analytic properties

## 4.1 Holomorphy

For fixed \(m\), the corrected whitened block

\[
\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m)
\]

is holomorphic in the microscopic disk. The projection

\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}
\]

is linear, and

\[
X\mapsto \omega_h(X,A_5^{\mathfrak f})
\]

is also linear for fixed \(m\). Therefore

\[
\boxed{
H_m^{\quot}(s)
\text{ is holomorphic wherever }
\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m)
\text{ is holomorphic.}
}
\]

So holomorphy survives.

## 4.2 Oddness

Oddness survives if the fixed-sector odd/even decomposition used for \(H_m\) survives before applying \(\Phi_K\). If even leakage is present, use the odd projection

\[
H_{m,\odd}^{\quot}(s)
=
\frac12
\left(
H_m^{\quot}(s)-H_m^{\quot}(-s)
\right).
\]

That is legitimate because the sampled odd-channel machinery already works with the odd Cauchy kernel. Thus oddness is not a fatal issue, but the quotient paper must explicitly define the odd quotient scalar, not rely on \(\Phi_K\)-oddness by inheritance.

## 4.3 Finite-order determinacy

If the fixed-sector quotient ideal is Noetherian, then finite determinacy applies to the sequence

\[
D_7,D_9,\ldots.
\]

Thus there is some finite depth \(R_{\Theta_k}^{\quot}\) such that

\[
D_7=D_9=\cdots=D_{2R_{\Theta_k}^{\quot}+1}=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
D_{2r+1}=0\quad\forall r.
\]

But this is only **quotient determinacy**:

\[
D_{2r+1}=0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}\in\mathbf C A_5^{\mathfrak f}.
\]

It is not representative-level vanishing.

## 4.4 Sampled-value reconstruction

The same odd Cauchy formula applies:

\[
H_m^{\quot}(s)
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}
\oint_{|\xi|=\rho_0/Q}
H_m^{\quot}(\xi)
\frac{2s}{\xi^2-s^2}\,d\xi.
\]

Therefore the sampled quantity

\[
\Xi_\zeta^{\quot,N}(m)
:=
\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}H_m^{\quot}(s_j)
\]

has the same kernel form:

\[
\Xi_\zeta^{\quot,N}(m)
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}
\oint_{|\xi|=\rho_0/Q}
H_m^{\quot}(\xi)K_{N,Q}(\xi)\,d\xi.
\]

So sampled reconstruction survives formally.

## 4.5 Boundary/error bounds

If

\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\]

is uniformly bounded in the same fixed-sector norm used for the corrected block, then

\[
|H_m^{\quot}(s)|
\le
\|A_5^{\mathfrak f}\|\,
\left\|
\pi_{\mathfrak f}\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m)
\right\|.
\]

Thus the old boundary estimate transfers with an extra factor depending on

\[
\|A_5^{\mathfrak f}\|.
\]

On a normalized good patch where

\[
\|A_5^{\mathfrak f}\|\asymp1
\]

or where \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\) is rescaled canonically, the same scale of estimates should persist.

However, without a fixed normalization, \(H_m^{\quot}\) is not scale-invariant:

\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}\mapsto \lambda A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
H_m^{\quot}\mapsto \lambda H_m^{\quot}.
\]

This is not a detection problem—zeros are unchanged—but it affects quantitative lower bounds such as

\[
\Lambda_{\quot}(m,Q).
\]

So the quotient pipeline requires a normalization convention, for example

\[
\|A_5^{\mathfrak f}\|=1,
\]

or a scale-invariant version

\[
\frac{
\omega_h(
\pi_{\mathfrak f}\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m),
A_5^{\mathfrak f})
}{
\|A_5^{\mathfrak f}\|
}.
\]

---

# 5. What fails: detection

The analytic pipeline mostly survives. The detection theorem does not.

The kernel of the quotient scalar is

\[
H_m^{\quot}\equiv0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\pi_{\mathfrak f}\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m)
\in
\mathbf C A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\]

for all odd coefficients, at least after finite determinacy.

That allows

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}
=
\lambda_{2r+1}A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\]

with nonzero \(\lambda_{2r+1}\).

Such a line anomaly is invisible to

\[
H_m^{\quot},
\]

but it is visible to

\[
\Phi_K
\]

whenever

\[
\Phi_K(A_5^{\mathfrak f})=2v_5\neq0.
\]

Indeed,

\[
\Phi_K(A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f})
=
\lambda_{2r+1}\Phi_K(A_5^{\mathfrak f})
=
2\lambda_{2r+1}v_5.
\]

So on the generic patch \(v_5\neq0\), the quotient scalar can vanish while the old transform scalar is nonzero.

For \(r=3\), this is exactly the lost \(T\)-direction:

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}
=
\lambda A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
D_7=0,
\]

but

\[
T=\frac{v_7}{c}
=
\lambda\frac{v_5}{c},
\]

which is nonzero if \(\lambda v_5\neq0\).

Thus

\[
\boxed{
H_m^{\quot}\equiv0
\text{ does not imply }
H_m\equiv0.
}
\]

Nor does it imply

\[
T=0.
\]

This is the central obstruction.

---

# 6. Direct countermodel

Take a good patch with

\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}=S,
\qquad
c\neq0.
\]

Then

\[
u_5=0,
\qquad
v_5=1.
\]

Let the entire odd fixed-sector anomaly be

\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m)
=
\lambda(s)S,
\]

where

\[
\lambda(s)
=
\lambda_5s^5+\lambda_7s^7+\cdots
\]

is nonzero.

Then

\[
H_m^{\quot}(s)
=
\omega_h(\lambda(s)S,S)
=
0.
\]

But

\[
H_m(s)
=
\Phi_K(\lambda(s)S)
=
2\lambda(s),
\]

which is nonzero.

In particular,

\[
D_{2r+1}=0
\quad\forall r,
\]

but

\[
\Phi_K(A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f})=2\lambda_{2r+1}
\]

can be nonzero.

This model proves:

\[
\boxed{
H_m^{\quot}
\text{ cannot be a drop-in replacement for }
H_m.
}
\]

It can only replace \(H_m\) after proving that pure \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\)-line anomalies are RH-irrelevant or lie in a controlled incidence set.

---

# 7. Does \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\)-dependence cause analytic failure?

Not primarily.

There are three technical dependencies:

1. \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}=A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)\) depends on \(m\).
2. It depends on normalization/whitening.
3. It may vanish or become singular on bad patches.

For fixed \(m\), none of these destroys holomorphy in \(s\). They matter when differentiating in \(m\), comparing patches, or proving uniform lower bounds.

The quotient architecture must therefore add good-patch hypotheses:

\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}\neq0,
\]

\[
\|A_5^{\mathfrak f}\|\asymp1
\quad\text{or fixed normalization},
\]

and exclusion of the locus where the quotient line is ill-defined.

These are manageable. They are not the main problem. The main problem is loss of the line component.

---

# 8. Replacement pipeline if the missing theorem were proved

If one could prove

\[
\boxed{
\text{RH-relevant anomaly}
\Rightarrow
H_m^{\quot}\not\equiv0,
}
\]

then the quotient replacement pipeline would be:

1. Define

\[
H_m^{\quot}(s)
=
\omega_h(
\pi_{\mathfrak f}\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m),
A_5^{\mathfrak f}).
\]

2. Take its odd part and expansion:

\[
H_m^{\quot}(s)
=
\sum_{r\ge0}D_{2r+1}s^{2r+1}.
\]

3. Use quotient finite determinacy:

\[
D_7=\cdots=D_{2R+1}=0
\Rightarrow
D_{2r+1}=0\quad\forall r.
\]

4. Use quotient finite-core sampling:

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\quot}
=
(D_7,D_9,\ldots,D_{2R+1})
=
P_{\rm fd}^{\quot}+J_{\rm fd}^{\quot}.
\]

5. Prove quotient sampled non-concentration:

\[
\theta(m)\notin
\mathcal Z_k^{\quot}\cup\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\quot}
\Rightarrow
\|P_{\rm fd}^{\quot}+J_{\rm fd}^{\quot}\|_\infty
\ge
\Lambda_{\quot}(m,Q).
\]

6. Close quotient interaction/incidence branches.

But step 0 is missing:

\[
H_m^{\quot}\equiv0
\quad\Rightarrow\quad
\text{no RH-relevant anomaly}.
\]

Without that, the pipeline detects only quotient-transverse anomalies, not all possible off-line anomalies.

---

# 9. No-go statement

**Proposition — Quotient scalar readout is analytically coherent but not RH-detecting without a line-gauge theorem.**  
Let

\[
H_m^{\quot}(s)
=
\omega_h(
\pi_{\mathfrak f}\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m),
A_5^{\mathfrak f})
\]

and suppose the odd fixed-sector expansion is

\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m)
=
\sum_{r\ge0}
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}s^{2r+1}.
\]

Then

\[
H_m^{\quot}(s)
=
\sum_{r\ge0}
D_{2r+1}s^{2r+1},
\qquad
D_{2r+1}
=
\omega_h(A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}).
\]

Thus \(H_m^{\quot}\) is holomorphic and supports the same odd Cauchy sampling machinery as \(H_m\), after fixed normalization of \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\).

However,

\[
H_m^{\quot}\equiv0
\]

implies only

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}\in\mathbf C A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\quad\forall r,
\]

not

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}=0,
\qquad
\Phi_K(A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f})=0,
\qquad
T=0.
\]

On the generic patch

\[
\Phi_K(A_5^{\mathfrak f})=2v_5\neq0,
\]

there exist nonzero line anomalies

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}
=
\lambda_{2r+1}A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\]

with

\[
H_m^{\quot}\equiv0
\]

but

\[
\Phi_K(A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f})
=
2\lambda_{2r+1}v_5\neq0.
\]

Therefore \(H_m^{\quot}\) cannot replace \(H_m\) unless one proves that such line anomalies are RH-irrelevant or contained in the quotient geometric exceptional set.

---

# 10. Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
H_m^{\quot}
\text{ can replace the analytic sampling machinery of }H_m,
\text{ but not its RH-detection content.}
}
\]

More precisely:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Holomorphy, odd sampling, and finite quotient determinacy survive.}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{Detection of line-gauge / }T\text{-visible anomalies is lost.}
}
\]

So the indispensable missing theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Pure }A_5^{\mathfrak f}\text{-line anomalies are not RH-relevant,}
}
\]

or equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\text{Every RH-relevant off-line anomaly has nonzero quotient determinant data.}
}
\]

Until that theorem is proved, the no-\(T\) quotient scalar pipeline remains coherent but incomplete, and Branch C remains at a kill switch rather than an active proof route.
## User (2026-04-25 17:54:42)

You are Agent 5. Do your attack.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 17:54:55)

_Thought for 12s_

# Agent 5 — Split Finite-Core / Interaction Architecture Agent

## Verdict

The split readout

\[
\mathcal H_m^{\mathrm{split}}(s)
=
\left(
H_m^{\quot}(s),
H_m^{\parallel}(s)
\right)
\]

is **complete on a good patch** if the whitening inner product is canonical and nondegenerate. It fixes the pure quotient failure because it detects both quotient-transverse and \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\)-line anomalies.

But this also means it essentially reconstructs the full fixed-sector odd germ. Therefore it does **not** automatically solve Bottleneck C; it reintroduces the longitudinal direction that the quotient architecture discarded. The split architecture is stronger than pure quotient, but finite-core non-concentration and \(dd\)-interaction rigidity remain at least as hard as in the full vector architecture. The attached instruction frames this exact target: define the split finite-core/interaction architecture and decide whether it escapes both the old \(\Phi_K\)-scalar obstruction and the quotient lost-\(T\) failure. 

---

# 1. Split finite-core vector

For each odd fixed-sector coefficient,

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}
=
u_{2r+1}I+v_{2r+1}S,
\]

define

\[
D_{2r+1}
=
\omega_h(A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f})
=
u_{2r+1}v_5-u_5v_{2r+1},
\]

and

\[
\lambda_{2r+1}
=
\frac{
\langle A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}\rangle_{\wh}
}{
\langle A_5^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}\rangle_{\wh}
}.
\]

Then define

\[
\boxed{
C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}
=
(D_7,\lambda_7,D_9,\lambda_9,\ldots,D_{2R+1},\lambda_{2R+1}).
}
\]

This is the natural finite-core vector for the split readout.

---

# 2. Zero loci comparison

Define

\[
\mathcal Z_k^{\mathrm{split}}
=
\{\theta:C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}(\theta)=0\}.
\]

Compare:

\[
\mathcal Z_k
=
\{\theta:A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}=0\ \forall r\},
\]

\[
\mathcal Z_k^{\quot}
=
\{\theta:D_{2r+1}=0\ \forall r\}.
\]

On a good patch where:

\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}\neq0,
\]

\[
\langle A_5^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}\rangle_{\wh}\neq0,
\]

and the pair of functionals

\[
A\mapsto \omega_h(A,A_5^{\mathfrak f}),
\qquad
A\mapsto
\frac{\langle A,A_5^{\mathfrak f}\rangle_{\wh}}
{\langle A_5^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}\rangle_{\wh}}
\]

separates \(\mathfrak f_h\), we have:

\[
\boxed{
D_{2r+1}=0,\ \lambda_{2r+1}=0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}=0.
}
\]

Therefore, on such patches,

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Z_k^{\mathrm{split}}=\mathcal Z_k.
}
\]

By contrast,

\[
\mathcal Z_k\subseteq \mathcal Z_k^{\quot},
\]

with strict inclusion generically, because quotient data miss line anomalies

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}
=
\lambda_{2r+1}A_5^{\mathfrak f}.
\]

So the split architecture corrects the quotient architecture’s lost-\(T\) defect.

---

# 3. Finite determinacy

If the full fixed-sector finite determinacy theorem holds for

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f},
\]

then split finite determinacy follows immediately on a good patch, because

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}
\longleftrightarrow
(D_{2r+1},\lambda_{2r+1})
\]

is an invertible linear coordinate change.

Thus the desired split theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
D_7=\lambda_7=D_9=\lambda_9=\cdots=D_{2R+1}=\lambda_{2R+1}=0
}
\]

implies

\[
\boxed{
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}=0\quad\forall r.
}
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
H_{\core,\theta}^{\mathfrak f}\equiv0.
}
\]

This is not a new miracle theorem. It is essentially the full finite-determinacy theorem expressed in split coordinates.

---

# 4. Split pair / interaction decomposition

Write

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}
=
P_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}
+
J_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}},
\]

where

\[
P_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}
=
(D^{P}_7,\lambda^{P}_7,D^{P}_9,\lambda^{P}_9,\ldots),
\]

and

\[
J_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}
=
(D^{J}_7,\lambda^{J}_7,D^{J}_9,\lambda^{J}_9,\ldots).
\]

Since the split coordinates are linear in the fixed-sector coefficients, the old decomposition transfers componentwise.

The problem is that the old alignment branch transfers too:

\[
J_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}
\approx
-
P_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}.
\]

So the sampled non-concentration theorem becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\theta(m)\notin
\mathcal Z_k^{\mathrm{split}}\cup\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\mathrm{split}}
\Longrightarrow
\|P_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}+J_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}\|_\infty
\ge
\Lambda_{\mathrm{split}}(m,Q).
}
\]

But because

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}
\]

is equivalent to the full fixed-sector vector, this theorem is not obviously easier than the old full-vector non-concentration theorem.

In fact:

\[
\boxed{
\text{split non-concentration is essentially full non-concentration in better coordinates.}
}
\]

It avoids the quotient lost-line problem, but it does not remove the cancellation problem.

---

# 5. Interaction feature curve

The split feature curve should be vector-valued:

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u)
=
\left(
v_{\quot}(u),
v_{\parallel}(u)
\right),
\]

where

\[
v_{\quot}(u)
\]

is the quotient-transverse feature and

\[
v_{\parallel}(u)
\]

is the longitudinal \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\)-line feature induced by the \(\lambda\)-component.

Then the split \(dd\)-interaction kernel has the schematic form

\[
\boxed{
K_{ij}^{dd,\mathrm{split}}
=
(u_i-u_j)^2
\left\langle
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_i),
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_j)
\right\rangle_{\mathrm{split}}.
}
\]

If the split metric is block diagonal, this is

\[
K_{ij}^{dd,\mathrm{split}}
=
(u_i-u_j)^2
\left(
\langle v_{\quot}(u_i),v_{\quot}(u_j)\rangle
+
\langle v_{\parallel}(u_i),v_{\parallel}(u_j)\rangle
\right).
\]

The balanced feature condition becomes

\[
\sum_i a_i v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_i)=0
\qquad
\text{for all }
\sum_i a_i=0.
\]

As before,

\[
\boxed{
\sum_i a_i v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_i)=0
\text{ for all }a\in\mathbf 1^\perp
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_1)=\cdots=v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_k).
}
\]

So feature balance still requires constancy of the split feature curve on the sampled finite core.

The quotient countermodel lifts immediately:

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u)=v_0+\varepsilon u^2w,
\]

with

\[
u=(-1,0,1),
\qquad
a=(1,-2,1),
\]

gives

\[
\sum_i a_i v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_i)=2\varepsilon w\neq0.
\]

Therefore the split architecture does **not** automatically solve interaction feature balance. It merely gives a more complete feature space.

---

# 6. Spectral cone status

The split spectral kernel is

\[
K_{dd}^{\mathrm{split}}(t;m)
=
t^2
\left\langle
v_{\mathrm{split}}\!\left(m+\frac t2\right),
v_{\mathrm{split}}\!\left(m-\frac t2\right)
\right\rangle_{\mathrm{split}}.
\]

For a unit-normalized split feature curve,

\[
\langle v_{\mathrm{split}}(u),v_{\mathrm{split}}(u)\rangle_{\mathrm{split}}=1,
\]

the same expansion gives

\[
\kappa_4
=
-\frac12
\langle v_1,v_1\rangle
\le0,
\]

but

\[
\kappa_6
=
-\frac{1}{24}
\langle v_1,v_3\rangle.
\]

So the same unresolved sign condition remains:

\[
\boxed{
\langle v_1,v_3\rangle\le0.
}
\]

A unit split feature curve

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u)
=
(\cos(au+bu^3),\sin(au+bu^3))
\]

inside any two-dimensional split subspace still violates the desired sign when

\[
b>\frac{a^3}{6}.
\]

Thus the spectral cone problem survives unless the split geometry imposes additional curvature restrictions on \(v_{\mathrm{split}}\). I do not see such restrictions from the split itself.

---

# 7. Does split avoid lost \(T\)?

Yes, conditionally.

The split readout detects pure line anomalies because if

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}
=
\lambda_{2r+1}A_5^{\mathfrak f},
\]

then

\[
D_{2r+1}=0,
\]

but

\[
\lambda_{2r+1}\neq0.
\]

So the lost-\(T\) failure of the quotient scalar is fixed.

In that sense:

\[
\boxed{
\text{split readout is complete where pure quotient readout is incomplete.}
}
\]

---

# 8. Does split avoid old Bottleneck C?

Not by itself.

The old Bottleneck-C obstruction lived in an \(S\)-visible line component. The split readout keeps this component as \(\lambda\), instead of discarding it.

That means the \(\eta\eta\) top row now appears in the longitudinal channel unless a new line-bundle transport law says nonzero longitudinal motion is acceptable.

If the split architecture still requires

\[
\lambda(E_{\eta\eta})=0
\]

pointwise, then it has simply renamed the old obstruction.

On a good patch, since the map

\[
E=eI+fS
\mapsto
(D(E),\lambda(E))
\]

is invertible, requiring both components of the Bottleneck-C residual to vanish is equivalent to

\[
E=0,
\]

which is stronger than the old \(f=0\) condition.

So the split architecture can only improve the situation if it changes the proof gate:

\[
\boxed{
\text{do not kill }\lambda\text{ pointwise;}
\quad
\text{transport or control it canonically.}
}
\]

That belongs to the line-bundle connection program, not to finite-core algebra alone.

---

# 9. Comparison of architectures

| Architecture | Detects transverse anomaly? | Detects line anomaly? | Avoids Bottleneck-C \(T\)-leak? | Interaction easier? | Status |
|---|---:|---:|---:|---:|---|
| Old \(\Phi_K\) scalar | Partly, via \(S\) | Yes | No | No | Blocked |
| Pure quotient \(H^{\quot}\) | Yes | No | Yes by discarding it | No | Incomplete |
| Split readout \((D,\lambda)\) | Yes | Yes | Only if line transport replaces pointwise vanishing | No | Coherent but blocked |
| Full fixed-sector vector | Yes | Yes | Same as split | No | Equivalent to split |

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{split readout is essentially the full fixed-sector vector in canonical coordinates.}
}
\]

It fixes quotient incompleteness, but it inherits the full-vector Bottleneck and interaction burdens.

---

# 10. Theorem dependency graph for split architecture

To make the split architecture viable, the paper would need:

### Theorem 1 — Canonical split

\[
\mathfrak f_h
=
\mathbf C A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\oplus
(A_5^{\mathfrak f})^{\perp_{\wh}}
\]

is canonical and patch-compatible.

### Theorem 2 — Split finite determinacy

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}=0
\Rightarrow
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}=0\quad\forall r.
\]

This likely follows from full finite determinacy plus coordinate invertibility.

### Theorem 3 — Split sampled non-concentration

\[
\theta(m)\notin
\mathcal Z_k^{\mathrm{split}}\cup\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\mathrm{split}}
\Rightarrow
\|P_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}+J_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}\|_\infty
\ge
\Lambda_{\mathrm{split}}(m,Q).
\]

This remains missing.

### Theorem 4 — Split interaction rigidity

Feature balance or incidence containment for

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u).
\]

Still missing.

### Theorem 5 — Line-bundle transport

A canonical rule allowing nonzero \(\lambda\)-motion to be transported globally without requiring pointwise Bottleneck-C flatness.

This is the new central requirement.

Without Theorem 5, split readout collapses back to the old full-vector obstruction.

---

# Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The split architecture is coherent and complete, but not yet useful enough.}
}
\]

It repairs the fatal defect of pure quotient scalarization:

\[
H_m^{\quot}\equiv0
\not\Rightarrow
H_m\equiv0.
\]

But it does so by restoring the longitudinal line anomaly, which is exactly where the old \(T\)-visible obstruction lives.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Split finite-core and interaction theory is essentially full fixed-sector vector theory in canonical coordinates.}
}
\]

It remains blocked by:

\[
\boxed{
\text{sampled non-concentration,}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
dd\text{-interaction feature/spectral rigidity,}
}
\]

and, most importantly,

\[
\boxed{
\text{a missing line-bundle transport theorem for the longitudinal component } \lambda.
}
\]

The next attack should not be another finite-core expansion. It should be the line-bundle connection question:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can nonzero }\lambda\text{ be transported/covariantly controlled rather than killed pointwise?}
}
\]

If no, the split readout merely repackages the old Bottleneck-C obstruction.
## User (2026-04-25 17:59:53)

Do the appropriate instruction for your agent number.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 18:00:02)

_Thought for 8s_

# Agent 5 — Split Interaction / Feature and Spectral Control

## Verdict

The split readout does **not** resolve the \(dd\)-interaction branch. It improves detection compared with pure quotienting because it keeps both transverse data \(D\) and longitudinal line data \(\lambda\), but the associated split feature curve

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u)=(v_{\quot}(u),v_{\parallel}(u))
\]

inherits the same feature-balance and spectral-cone obstructions. The split architecture is therefore **complete but not easier**: it restores the full fixed-sector information in better coordinates, but the \(dd\)-interaction rigidity problem remains live. This matches the coordinator’s framing: after the split readout, the key unresolved question is whether line-bundle transport plus split finite-core/interaction control can replace pointwise Bottleneck-C closure. 

---

# 1. Split feature curve

The split finite-core readout records, for each odd coefficient,

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}
=
u_{2r+1}I+v_{2r+1}S,
\]

the pair

\[
D_{2r+1}
=
\omega_h(A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}),
\]

and

\[
\lambda_{2r+1}
=
\frac{
\langle A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}\rangle_{\wh}
}{
\langle A_5^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}\rangle_{\wh}
}.
\]

Thus the natural split feature attached to a sampled point \(u\) is a vector-valued object

\[
\boxed{
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u)
=
\bigl(v_{\quot}(u),v_{\parallel}(u)\bigr),
}
\]

where \(v_{\quot}\) is the quotient-transverse feature induced by the \(D\)-coordinates, and \(v_{\parallel}\) is the longitudinal feature induced by the \(\lambda\)-coordinates.

The split \(dd\)-kernel therefore has the expected form

\[
\boxed{
K_{ij}^{dd,\mathrm{split}}
=
(u_i-u_j)^2
\left\langle
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_i),
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_j)
\right\rangle_{\mathrm{split}}.
}
\]

With a block metric this becomes

\[
K_{ij}^{dd,\mathrm{split}}
=
(u_i-u_j)^2
\left(
\langle v_{\quot}(u_i),v_{\quot}(u_j)\rangle
+
\langle v_{\parallel}(u_i),v_{\parallel}(u_j)\rangle
\right).
\]

---

# 2. Feature balance still requires constancy

Let

\[
a=(a_1,\ldots,a_k),
\qquad
\sum_i a_i=0.
\]

The split feature-balance condition is

\[
\sum_i a_i v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_i)=0
\qquad
\text{for all } a\in\mathbf 1^\perp.
\]

This is equivalent to

\[
\boxed{
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_1)=\cdots=v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_k).
}
\]

Proof: if all \(v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_i)\) are equal, the balanced sum vanishes. Conversely, choose \(a=e_i-e_j\). Then

\[
0=\sum_\ell a_\ell v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_\ell)
=
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_i)-v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_j),
\]

so all sampled split features coincide.

Thus the split architecture has not weakened the feature-balance theorem. It still needs sampled constancy of a feature curve on the finite core.

---

# 3. Split geometry does not force constancy

The split readout gives more complete coordinates, but completeness does not imply constancy. A generic split feature curve varies.

The standard three-point countermodel survives:

\[
u=(-1,0,1),
\qquad
a=(1,-2,1),
\]

and

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u)
=
v_0+\varepsilon u^2w,
\]

where \(w\neq0\) is a split-feature direction. Then

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}(-1)=v_0+\varepsilon w,
\]

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}(0)=v_0,
\]

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}(1)=v_0+\varepsilon w.
\]

Therefore

\[
\sum_i a_i v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_i)
=
(v_0+\varepsilon w)-2v_0+(v_0+\varepsilon w)
=
2\varepsilon w\neq0.
\]

This violates feature balance.

Nothing in the split definition forces this model into collision, quotient degeneracy, longitudinal degeneracy, whitening singularity, or patch boundary. It is a regular finite-dimensional feature variation unless a separate incidence theorem forbids the quadratic split-feature direction \(w\).

So the live obstruction is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{feature-balance failure can occur outside known incidence purely from split-feature variation.}
}
\]

---

# 4. Spectral cone calculation

The split spectral kernel is

\[
K_{dd}^{\mathrm{split}}(t;m)
=
t^2
\left\langle
v_{\mathrm{split}}\left(m+\frac t2\right),
v_{\mathrm{split}}\left(m-\frac t2\right)
\right\rangle_{\mathrm{split}}.
\]

Write

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}(m+h)
=
v_0+h v_1+\frac{h^2}{2}v_2+\frac{h^3}{6}v_3
+\frac{h^4}{24}v_4+O(h^5).
\]

Then

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}\left(m+\frac t2\right)
=
v_0+\frac t2v_1+\frac{t^2}{8}v_2+\frac{t^3}{48}v_3+\frac{t^4}{384}v_4+O(t^5),
\]

and

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}\left(m-\frac t2\right)
=
v_0-\frac t2v_1+\frac{t^2}{8}v_2-\frac{t^3}{48}v_3+\frac{t^4}{384}v_4+O(t^5).
\]

Taking the inner product gives

\[
\left\langle
v_{\mathrm{split}}\left(m+\frac t2\right),
v_{\mathrm{split}}\left(m-\frac t2\right)
\right\rangle
=
\kappa_2+\kappa_4t^2+\kappa_6t^4+O(t^6),
\]

so

\[
K_{dd}^{\mathrm{split}}(t;m)
=
\kappa_2t^2+\kappa_4t^4+\kappa_6t^6+O(t^8),
\]

with

\[
\kappa_2=\langle v_0,v_0\rangle,
\]

\[
\kappa_4=
\frac14
\left(
\langle v_0,v_2\rangle-\langle v_1,v_1\rangle
\right),
\]

and

\[
\kappa_6
=
\frac{1}{192}\langle v_0,v_4\rangle
-\frac{1}{48}\langle v_1,v_3\rangle
+\frac{1}{64}\langle v_2,v_2\rangle.
\]

Under unit split-feature normalization,

\[
\langle v_{\mathrm{split}}(u),v_{\mathrm{split}}(u)\rangle=1,
\]

differentiate twice:

\[
\langle v_1,v_1\rangle+\langle v_0,v_2\rangle=0,
\]

so

\[
\boxed{
\kappa_4
=
-\frac12\langle v_1,v_1\rangle
\le0.
}
\]

Differentiate four times:

\[
2\langle v_0,v_4\rangle
+
8\langle v_1,v_3\rangle
+
6\langle v_2,v_2\rangle
=0,
\]

so

\[
\langle v_0,v_4\rangle
=
-4\langle v_1,v_3\rangle
-3\langle v_2,v_2\rangle.
\]

Substituting into \(\kappa_6\),

\[
\kappa_6
=
\frac{1}{192}
\left(
-4\langle v_1,v_3\rangle
-3\langle v_2,v_2\rangle
\right)
-\frac{1}{48}\langle v_1,v_3\rangle
+\frac{1}{64}\langle v_2,v_2\rangle.
\]

The \(\langle v_2,v_2\rangle\) terms cancel:

\[
-\frac{3}{192}+\frac{1}{64}
=
-\frac{3}{192}+\frac{3}{192}
=
0.
\]

The \(\langle v_1,v_3\rangle\) terms combine:

\[
-\frac{4}{192}-\frac{1}{48}
=
-\frac{1}{48}-\frac{1}{48}
=
-\frac{1}{24}.
\]

Hence

\[
\boxed{
\kappa_6
=
-\frac{1}{24}\langle v_1,v_3\rangle.
}
\]

Therefore the same sign condition survives:

\[
\boxed{
\langle v_1,v_3\rangle\le0
}
\]

in the negative-type convention.

---

# 5. Unit-curve countermodel survives

Consider a two-dimensional split-feature subspace and the unit curve

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u)
=
(\cos\phi(u),\sin\phi(u)),
\]

with

\[
\phi(u)=au+bu^3.
\]

At \(u=0\),

\[
v_0=(1,0),
\]

\[
v_1=(0,a),
\]

\[
v_3=(-3a^2,6b-a^3).
\]

Thus

\[
\langle v_1,v_3\rangle
=
a(6b-a^3).
\]

For \(a>0\), this is positive precisely when

\[
\boxed{
b>\frac{a^3}{6}.
}
\]

Then

\[
\kappa_6
=
-\frac{1}{24}\langle v_1,v_3\rangle<0
\]

in the convention where the opposite sign is needed.

This curve is unit-normalized, smooth, and can live entirely inside the split feature space. It does not by itself trigger whitening singularity, line-gauge degeneracy, endpoint collision, or feature-rank drop.

Therefore spectral positivity is not automatic in the split architecture.

---

# 6. Does the split architecture improve interaction?

It improves **detection**, not **rigidity**.

Pure quotienting missed line anomalies. The split feature curve restores them:

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}=(v_{\quot},v_{\parallel}).
\]

But feature-balance and spectral-cone problems are insensitive to why the feature vector has its components. Once the feature curve is vector-valued, the same algebra applies.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{split readout fixes lost-}T\text{ incompleteness,}
}
\]

but

\[
\boxed{
\text{it does not supply feature balance or spectral positivity.}
}
\]

In fact, because split readout is equivalent to the full fixed-sector vector on good patches, the interaction branch is essentially the old full-vector interaction problem in canonical coordinates.

---

# 7. Required theorem if this route continues

The split architecture needs one of the following.

## Option A — Split feature-balance theorem

\[
\boxed{
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_1)=\cdots=v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_k)
}
\]

on every admissible same-scale finite core outside incidence.

This seems too strong and is not currently supported.

## Option B — Incidence containment

Feature-balance failure must imply controlled geometry:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_i a_i v_{\mathrm{split}}(u_i)\neq0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\theta\in\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\mathrm{split}}.
}
\]

Equivalently, the finite-difference defect must lie in the radical of the split incidence ideal.

The countermodel

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u)=v_0+\varepsilon u^2w
\]

shows this is not a formal consequence of split coordinates.

## Option C — Split spectral cone theorem

The kernel

\[
K_{dd}^{\mathrm{split}}
\]

must satisfy the required sign/negative-type condition on the balanced amplitude subspace, despite the local \(\kappa_6\) obstruction.

This would need a nonlocal cone theorem, not just unit normalization.

## Option D — Line-bundle transport absorbs interaction

The new line-bundle connection might impose extra curvature/transport constraints on \(v_{\parallel}\) that eliminate the bad \(v_1,v_3\) behavior. But this is not yet present. It belongs to the line-bundle transport wave, not to split feature algebra alone.

---

# 8. Final status

\[
\boxed{
\text{The split interaction branch remains open-negative.}
}
\]

More precisely:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Feature balance is still equivalent to sampled constancy of }
v_{\mathrm{split}},
}
\]

and split geometry does not force such constancy.

\[
\boxed{
\text{The spectral cone obstruction survives through }
\kappa_6=-\frac{1}{24}\langle v_1,v_3\rangle.
}
\]

The unit-curve model

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u)
=
(\cos(au+bu^3),\sin(au+bu^3)),
\qquad
b>\frac{a^3}{6},
\]

remains a valid local obstruction unless independently excluded by incidence or transport.

So the split readout is not a final architecture. It shifts the next necessary theorem to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{split feature-balance / spectral cone control / incidence containment.}
}
\]

Without such a theorem, the \(dd\)-interaction obstruction survives essentially unchanged.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 18:05:17)

# Agent 5 — Transported Finite-Core and Interaction Replacement

## Verdict

Replacing \(\lambda\) by \(\nabla^L\lambda\) does **not** by itself rescue the architecture. It permits local longitudinal motion formally, but it loses constant/parallel line anomalies unless one also supplies anchor data, boundary values, or holonomy. Once those are added back, the transported architecture becomes equivalent in strength to the split/full fixed-sector architecture, with the same sampled non-concentration and \(dd\)-interaction obstructions.

So the best possible transported finite-core object is not

\[
(D,\nabla^L\lambda)
\]

alone, but

\[
\boxed{
(D,\ \lambda_{\mathrm{anchor}},\ \nabla^L\lambda)
}
\]

or equivalently a holonomy/parallel-transported endpoint package. The attached prompt asks exactly whether finite-core and interaction should use \(\lambda\), \(\nabla^L\lambda\), or transported endpoint values. The answer is: \(\nabla^L\lambda\) alone is incomplete; transported endpoint data are complete but do not simplify the hard branches. 

---

## 1. Three candidate finite-core vectors

We compare:

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}
=
(D_7,\lambda_7,D_9,\lambda_9,\ldots),
\]

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\nabla}
=
(D_7,\nabla^L\lambda_7,D_9,\nabla^L\lambda_9,\ldots),
\]

and a holonomy/transported endpoint vector

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{Hol}}.
\]

Here

\[
D_{2r+1}
=
\omega_h(A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}),
\]

and

\[
\nabla^L\lambda_{2r+1}
=
d\lambda_{2r+1}
+
\lambda_{2r+1}\alpha_L.
\]

The split vector is complete on a good patch. The covariant-derivative vector is gauge-covariant but incomplete. The transported endpoint vector can be complete, but only by restoring the line data that \(\nabla^L\lambda\) forgets.

---

## 2. \(C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}\): complete but old obstruction returns

The split vector

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}
=
(D_7,\lambda_7,D_9,\lambda_9,\ldots)
\]

recovers the full fixed-sector data on a good patch, because \((D,\lambda)\) is an invertible coordinate system on

\[
\mathfrak f_h=\mathbf C I\oplus\mathbf C S.
\]

Thus

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}=0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}=0
\quad\text{for the controlled odd orders.}
\]

This is detection-complete. But it does not allow nonzero longitudinal Bottleneck-C forcing. If the local gate requires

\[
\lambda(E_{\eta\eta})=0,
\]

then the old obstruction returns. Since the active row contributes

\[
\lambda(E_{\eta\eta})
=
-\frac{\nu_{\eta\eta}}{24\pi q^3}
\frac{
\langle wI+uS,A_5^{\mathfrak f}\rangle_{\wh}
}{
\langle A_5^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}\rangle_{\wh}
},
\]

generic pointwise split closure again requires

\[
\nu_{\eta\eta}=0.
\]

So \(C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{split}}\) is complete but does not by itself solve Bottleneck C.

---

## 3. \(C_{\rm fd}^{\nabla}\): permits transport but loses constant line anomalies

Now consider

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\nabla}
=
(D_7,\nabla^L\lambda_7,D_9,\nabla^L\lambda_9,\ldots).
\]

This vector is compatible with the idea that \(\lambda\) need not vanish pointwise. It measures whether \(\lambda\) is transported according to the line connection.

But it has an immediate detection problem.

If

\[
\nabla^L\lambda_{2r+1}=0
\]

for every controlled order, then

\[
\lambda_{2r+1}
\]

is merely covariantly constant. It need not be zero.

Thus a pure line anomaly

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}
=
\lambda_{2r+1}A_5^{\mathfrak f},
\qquad
D_{2r+1}=0,
\qquad
\nabla^L\lambda_{2r+1}=0,
\]

can survive undetected by \(C_{\rm fd}^{\nabla}\) if \(\lambda_{2r+1}\neq0\) is parallel.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
C_{\rm fd}^{\nabla}=0
\not\Rightarrow
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}=0.
}
\]

It detects failure of transport, not nonzero transported line state.

So \(C_{\rm fd}^{\nabla}\) is not RH-detecting unless supplemented by boundary or anchor data.

---

## 4. \(C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{Hol}}\): complete only with endpoint/anchor data

A transported endpoint package would look like this. Choose an anchor point \(m_0\) or a canonical boundary section. Let

\[
\mathsf P_{m_0\to m}^L
\]

denote parallel transport in \(L\). Then compare

\[
\lambda_{2r+1}(m)
\]

to transported anchor data:

\[
\lambda_{2r+1}^{\mathrm{tr}}(m)
=
\mathsf P_{m_0\to m}^L\lambda_{2r+1}(m_0).
\]

Define the transported residual

\[
R_{2r+1}^{L}(m)
=
\lambda_{2r+1}(m)-\lambda_{2r+1}^{\mathrm{tr}}(m),
\]

or, in differential form,

\[
R_{2r+1}^{L}
=
\nabla^L\lambda_{2r+1}-F_{2r+1}^{L},
\]

if a prescribed forcing term \(F_{2r+1}^{L}\) is part of the architecture.

Then

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{Hol}}
=
(D_7,R_7^L,D_9,R_9^L,\ldots)
\]

can be complete only if the boundary/anchor values are fixed independently.

But this introduces a new theorem requirement:

\[
\boxed{
\text{There exists a canonical anchor, boundary condition, or holonomy constraint for }\lambda.
}
\]

Without such a boundary condition, transported nonzero \(\lambda\) remains invisible to the zero-condition.

---

## 5. Compatibility with nonzero Bottleneck-C forcing

If the architecture allows

\[
\lambda(E_{\eta\eta})\neq0,
\]

then the finite-core vector must not require \(\lambda=0\) pointwise. The natural replacement is a forced transport equation:

\[
\boxed{
\nabla^L\lambda_{2r+1}=F_{2r+1}^{L}.
}
\]

For Bottleneck C, the forcing contains

\[
F_{\eta\eta}^{L}
\sim
-\frac{\nu_{\eta\eta}}{24\pi q^3}
\frac{
\langle wI+uS,A_5^{\mathfrak f}\rangle_{\wh}
}{
\langle A_5^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}\rangle_{\wh}
}.
\]

Then the finite-core object should not include \(\lambda\) alone or \(\nabla^L\lambda\) alone. It should include the residual

\[
\boxed{
R_\lambda
=
\nabla^L\lambda-F^L.
}
\]

Thus a transported finite-core vector should be

\[
\boxed{
C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{tr}}
=
(D_7,R_7^L,D_9,R_9^L,\ldots),
}
\]

plus boundary/holonomy data for the line component.

This is the only formulation compatible with allowing nonzero Bottleneck-C longitudinal forcing.

However, this merely shifts the problem: one must prove that \(F^L\) is canonical, globally controlled, and has prescribed boundary/holonomy behavior. That theorem is not currently available.

---

## 6. Finite determinacy under transport

### With \(\lambda\)

\[
(D,\lambda)
\]

is complete, so finite determinacy follows from full fixed-sector determinacy.

### With \(\nabla^L\lambda\)

\[
(D,\nabla^L\lambda)
\]

is not complete because parallel nonzero line anomalies remain.

### With \((\lambda_{\mathrm{anchor}},\nabla^L\lambda)\)

This is complete along a path, since

\[
\lambda(m)
=
\mathsf P_{m_0\to m}^L\lambda(m_0)
+
\int_{m_0}^m
\mathsf P_{\tau\to m}^L
(\nabla^L\lambda)(\tau)\,d\tau.
\]

But this requires:

1. a canonical path,
2. a canonical anchor value,
3. good control of the integral,
4. path-independence or holonomy control.

Absent these, transported finite determinacy is not a local algebraic theorem; it becomes a global transport theorem.

---

## 7. Sampled non-concentration

Using \(\nabla^L\lambda\) does not improve sampled non-concentration.

The old obstruction was an aligned cancellation:

\[
J_{\rm fd}\approx -P_{\rm fd}.
\]

In transported variables this becomes

\[
J_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{tr}}\approx -P_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{tr}}.
\]

A formal model remains:

\[
P_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{tr}}=p,
\qquad
J_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{tr}}=-p+\varepsilon w,
\]

with

\[
0<
\|P_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{tr}}+J_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{tr}}\|_\infty
<
\Lambda_{\mathrm{tr}}.
\]

Unless the line transport forcing imposes a sign, monotonicity, or incidence condition, the aligned branch persists.

Thus the missing theorem becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal N_{k,\mathrm{tr}}^{\rm align}
\subseteq
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\mathrm{tr}},
}
\]

or an equivalent transported non-concentration theorem.

No automatic improvement follows from replacing \(\lambda\) by \(\nabla^L\lambda\).

---

## 8. Interaction feature curve under transport

There are two possible interaction features.

### Option A — use the split feature

\[
v_{\mathrm{split}}(u)
=
(v_{\quot}(u),v_{\parallel}(u)).
\]

Then all old feature-balance and spectral-cone obstructions survive.

### Option B — use the transported feature

\[
v_{\nabla}(u)
=
(v_{\quot}(u),\nabla^L v_{\parallel}(u)).
\]

This measures variation of the longitudinal component, not the component itself.

But \(v_{\nabla}\) again loses covariantly constant line anomalies unless boundary data are added. It also does not remove the feature-balance obstruction.

Feature balance would require

\[
\sum_i a_i v_{\nabla}(u_i)=0
\quad
\forall \sum_i a_i=0,
\]

equivalent to

\[
v_{\nabla}(u_1)=\cdots=v_{\nabla}(u_k).
\]

A countermodel survives:

\[
v_{\nabla}(u)
=
v_0+\varepsilon u^2w,
\qquad
u=(-1,0,1),
\qquad
a=(1,-2,1),
\]

giving

\[
\sum_i a_i v_{\nabla}(u_i)=2\varepsilon w\neq0.
\]

So transport does not eliminate feature-balance failure.

---

## 9. Spectral cone under transport

For

\[
K_{dd}^{\nabla}(t;m)
=
t^2
\left\langle
v_{\nabla}\left(m+\frac t2\right),
v_{\nabla}\left(m-\frac t2\right)
\right\rangle,
\]

the same expansion gives

\[
\kappa_4
=
-\frac12\langle v_1,v_1\rangle
\le0
\]

under unit normalization, but

\[
\kappa_6
=
-\frac{1}{24}\langle v_1,v_3\rangle.
\]

The same unit-curve countermodel

\[
v_{\nabla}(u)=(\cos(au+bu^3),\sin(au+bu^3))
\]

violates the required sign when

\[
b>\frac{a^3}{6}.
\]

Thus spectral cone control also survives as a separate missing theorem.

---

## 10. Resulting architecture if one insists on transport

The minimal coherent transported architecture is:

\[
\boxed{
C_{\rm fd}^{\mathrm{transport}}
=
(D_7,R_7^L,D_9,R_9^L,\ldots;\ \lambda_{\mathrm{anchor}})
}
\]

where

\[
R_{2r+1}^L
=
\nabla^L\lambda_{2r+1}
-
F_{2r+1}^L.
\]

This requires:

1. canonical line connection \(\nabla^L\);
2. canonical forcing \(F_{2r+1}^L\);
3. canonical anchor/boundary/holonomy condition;
4. transported finite determinacy;
5. transported sampled non-concentration;
6. transported interaction rigidity.

This is a genuine new global transport program. It is not a local repair.

---

## Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Using }\nabla^L\lambda\text{ alone is not viable.}
}
\]

It loses constant/parallel line anomalies.

\[
\boxed{
\text{Using transported endpoint or residual data can be complete,}
}
\]

but only with a canonical boundary/holonomy theorem.

\[
\boxed{
\text{Transport does not improve finite-core sampled non-concentration or }dd\text{-interaction rigidity by itself.}
}
\]

The best possible architecture is therefore:

\[
\boxed{
(D,\ R^L=\nabla^L\lambda-F^L,\ \lambda_{\rm boundary/holonomy})
}
\]

with a new global line-bundle theorem. Without that theorem, line transport merely shifts the old Bottleneck-C obstruction into a forced longitudinal equation and leaves the downstream interaction/non-concentration problems intact.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 18:22:16)

_Thought for 14s_

# Agent 5 — Energy / Positivity Invariant Agent

## Verdict

I do **not** find a viable energy/positivity replacement for Bottleneck C. The natural fixed-sector energies are coherent and detect the bad row, but the \(\eta\eta\) contribution is either **sign-indefinite at first variation** or **tautologically positive at second variation** without any global theorem forcing the energy to vanish. Therefore the obstruction cannot presently be converted into a useful positivity contradiction. This matches the attached wave’s strategic reset: Gate A/B remain useful local algebraic lemmas, but the failed Bottleneck-C scalar/quotient/split/line-bundle family needs a genuinely new invariant, not another pointwise closure patch. 

---

## 1. Natural fixed-sector energy

Let

\[
V_m=\mathfrak f_h(m)=\mathbf C I\oplus \mathbf C S
\]

with the whitened Hermitian metric

\[
\langle\cdot,\cdot\rangle_{\wh}.
\]

The most natural full fixed-sector energy is

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_7
=
\|A_7^{\mathfrak f}\|_{\wh}^2.
}
\]

Using the split decomposition relative to

\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f},
\]

one can also write, on a good patch,

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}
=
A_7^\perp+\lambda_7A_5^{\mathfrak f},
\]

so

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_{\rm full}
=
\|A_7^\perp\|_{\wh}^2
+
|\lambda_7|^2\|A_5^{\mathfrak f}\|_{\wh}^2.
}
\]

Equivalently, after normalizing the quotient determinant,

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_{\rm full}
\sim
\frac{|D_7|^2}{\|A_5^{\mathfrak f}\|_{\wh}^2}
+
|\lambda_7|^2\|A_5^{\mathfrak f}\|_{\wh}^2.
}
\]

This fixes the pure quotient incompleteness: both transverse and longitudinal defects are counted.

A higher-order version is

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_{\odd}
=
\sum_{r\ge 3} \omega_r
\|A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}\|_{\wh}^2
}
\]

for positive weights \(\omega_r\).

---

## 2. Bottleneck-C row contribution

The active bad row is

\[
E_{\eta\eta}
=
-\frac{\nu_{\eta\eta}}{24\pi q^3}(wI+uS).
\]

Write

\[
B:=wI+uS,
\qquad
\gamma:=-\frac{\nu_{\eta\eta}}{24\pi q^3},
\]

so

\[
E_{\eta\eta}=\gamma B.
\]

For the energy

\[
\mathcal E_7=\|A_7^{\mathfrak f}\|_{\wh}^2,
\]

a perturbation

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}\mapsto A_7^{\mathfrak f}+\varepsilon E_{\eta\eta}
\]

gives

\[
\mathcal E_7(\varepsilon)
=
\|A_7^{\mathfrak f}\|_{\wh}^2
+
2\operatorname{Re}
\left[
\varepsilon
\langle A_7^{\mathfrak f},E_{\eta\eta}\rangle_{\wh}
\right]
+
|\varepsilon|^2
\|E_{\eta\eta}\|_{\wh}^2.
\]

Thus the first variation is

\[
\boxed{
\delta\mathcal E_7
=
2\operatorname{Re}
\left[
\gamma
\langle A_7^{\mathfrak f},B\rangle_{\wh}
\right].
}
\]

The second variation is

\[
\boxed{
\delta^2\mathcal E_7
=
2|\gamma|^2\|B\|_{\wh}^2
\ge 0.
}
\]

So the row is detectable by energy, but the first variation has no fixed sign unless the architecture supplies a strong alignment/sign theorem for

\[
\operatorname{Re}
\left[
\gamma
\langle A_7^{\mathfrak f},B\rangle_{\wh}
\right].
\]

No such theorem is present.

---

## 3. Quotient/parallel energy split

For the split components,

\[
D(E_{\eta\eta})
=
\omega_h(E_{\eta\eta},A_5^{\mathfrak f}),
\]

and

\[
\lambda(E_{\eta\eta})
=
\frac{
\langle E_{\eta\eta},A_5^{\mathfrak f}\rangle_{\wh}
}{
\langle A_5^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}\rangle_{\wh}
}.
\]

The split energy contribution is

\[
\boxed{
\|E_{\eta\eta}\|_{\wh}^2
=
\|E_{\eta\eta}^{\perp}\|_{\wh}^2
+
|\lambda(E_{\eta\eta})|^2
\|A_5^{\mathfrak f}\|_{\wh}^2.
}
\]

This is positive. But positivity alone is not a contradiction. It says only:

\[
E_{\eta\eta}\neq0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\|E_{\eta\eta}\|_{\wh}^2>0.
\]

To turn this into a proof gate, one would need a global theorem forcing

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm full}=0
\]

or forcing a monotone quantity to be both nonnegative and zero at endpoints. The previous line-bundle wave found no canonical boundary/holonomy condition of that kind. The energy formulation does not supply it.

---

## 4. Does the \(\eta\eta\) row have a useful sign?

The coefficient

\[
\nu_{\eta\eta}
\]

has the rebuilt aggregate form

\[
\nu_{21,7}^{\new,\agg}
=
\frac{1}{Q^{14}}
\left[
\frac{q_0^{\new}}{240}A_2^{\new}A_4^{\new}
+
\frac{1}{144}
\left(
\frac{(q_0^{\new})''}{6}
-
\frac{2(q_0^{\new})^3}{3}
\right)
(A_2^{\new})^2
\right].
\]

This expression is not sign-definite. For example, if

\[
A_4^{\new}=0,
\qquad
(q_0^{\new})''=0,
\qquad
q_0^{\new}>0,
\]

then

\[
\nu_{21,7}^{\new,\agg}
=
-\frac{(q_0^{\new})^3}{216Q^{14}}
(A_2^{\new})^2<0.
\]

But other choices of \(A_4^{\new}/A_2^{\new}\) can change the sign. Therefore the scalar coefficient driving the row is indefinite.

Squaring it gives

\[
|\nu_{\eta\eta}|^2,
\]

which is nonnegative, but then the proof needs a reason this nonnegative quantity must vanish. That is exactly the missing global constraint in another form.

---

## 5. Candidate monotonicity formula fails locally

A useful monotonicity formula would need something like

\[
\frac{d}{dm}\mathcal E_{\odd}(m)
=
\text{definite expression involving } \nu_{\eta\eta},
\]

or

\[
\frac{d^2}{dm^2}\mathcal E_{\odd}(m)
\ge 0
\]

with boundary values forcing equality.

But the known exactness calculation for the obstruction gives, in fixed-source notation,

\[
2880Q^{14}\nu
=
qAA''
+
20\left(\frac{q''}{6}-\frac{2q^3}{3}\right)A^2.
\]

Integration by parts yields

\[
qAA''
+
20\left(\frac{q''}{6}-\frac{2q^3}{3}\right)A^2
=
\left(qAA'-\frac12q'A^2\right)'
-
q(A')^2
+
\left(
\frac{23}{6}q''
-
\frac{40}{3}q^3
\right)A^2.
\]

The bulk terms are not sign-controlled in the required way:

\[
-q(A')^2
+
\left(
\frac{23}{6}q''
-
\frac{40}{3}q^3
\right)A^2.
\]

In the usual good-patch regime with \(q>0\) and \(q''\) not dominating \(q^3\), this is negative, not a positive coercive energy. Reversing the sign gives a positive-looking term only by changing the direction of the would-be monotonicity, and still leaves no endpoint theorem.

So the obstruction is not naturally the derivative of a nonnegative energy.

---

## 6. Model showing sign indefiniteness

Let the whitened metric be standard on the fixed sector and take

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}=B=wI+uS.
\]

Then

\[
\delta\mathcal E_7
=
2\operatorname{Re}(\gamma)\|B\|^2.
\]

Changing the source moments so that

\[
\nu_{\eta\eta}\mapsto -\nu_{\eta\eta}
\]

changes

\[
\gamma\mapsto-\gamma,
\]

and hence flips the sign of the energy variation.

Thus the first variation cannot be globally positive or globally negative without a sign theorem for \(\nu_{\eta\eta}\). The current framework has none; indeed previous adversarial models specifically vary the ratio \(A_4/A_2\).

---

## 7. Energy does not avoid finite-core/interaction issues

Even if one uses

\[
\mathcal E_{\odd}
=
\sum_r \omega_r\|A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}\|_{\wh}^2,
\]

the downstream problem becomes a normed version of full vector non-concentration:

\[
\theta(m)\notin\mathcal Z_k^{\vec f}\cup\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\vec f}
\Longrightarrow
\sum_r \omega_r
\|P_{2r+1}^{\vec f}+J_{2r+1}^{\vec f}\|_{\wh}^2
\ge
\Lambda_{\vec f}(m,Q)^2.
\]

But the aligned branch remains:

\[
J_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}\approx -P_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}.
\]

The norm does not prevent cancellation before the norm is taken.

Similarly, the \(dd\)-interaction feature/spectral countermodels persist. Energy can measure their size, but it does not force the feature-balance or spectral-cone signs needed to eliminate them.

---

## 8. What a positive energy route would require

A viable energy replacement would need all of the following:

1. A canonical positive energy

\[
\mathcal E(m)\ge0.
\]

2. A theorem tying RH-compatible configurations to zero energy:

\[
\mathcal E(m)=0
\iff
\text{RH-compatible local state}.
\]

3. A global identity or boundary condition forcing

\[
\mathcal E(m)=0
\]

or

\[
\int \mathcal E(m)\,dm=0.
\]

4. A proof that off-line zeros force

\[
\mathcal E(m)>0
\]

on some good patch.

The current architecture gives only item 1 and possibly item 4. It does not give item 2 or item 3.

Without item 3, positivity is descriptive, not contradictory.

---

# Final conclusion

\[
\boxed{
\text{The energy route is coherent but not viable as a proof gate.}
}
\]

The bad \(\eta\eta\) row contributes nontrivially to fixed-sector energy, but:

\[
\boxed{
\text{first variations are sign-indefinite,}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{second variations are positive but tautological,}
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
\text{no global zero-energy, monotonicity, or boundary theorem is available.}
}
\]

Therefore the energy/positivity invariant does not replace pointwise Bottleneck-C \(T\)-flatness. It repackages the obstruction as nonzero norm rather than eliminating or globally controlling it.

Gate A and Gate B should still be kept as local algebraic lemmas, but the energy route does not provide the missing local-to-global proof mechanism.

_Thought for 6s_

# Agent 5 — Derived Projective/Energy Invariants from Rank-Two Transport

## Verdict

Assuming the rank-two fixed-sector bundle \(\mathcal F\) and a local connection \(\nabla^{\mathfrak f}\) exist, the derived projective and energy invariants are useful **diagnostics**, but I do **not** find a proof gate. Projective invariants still miss pure line/scale anomalies unless paired with scale data; energy detects the Bottleneck-C row but remains sign-indefinite or tautologically positive; affine displacement norms require canonical paths and boundary conditions that are not yet available. This matches the current wave’s premise: the rank-two transport route is the least-bad remaining architecture, but it needs a genuine global affine transport theorem, not another scalar invariant. 

---

# 1. Candidate invariant package

Assume a rank-two bundle

\[
\mathcal F\to\mathcal B,
\qquad
\mathcal F_b=\mathfrak f_h(b)=\mathbf C I_b\oplus \mathbf C S_b,
\]

with a connection

\[
\nabla^{\mathfrak f}.
\]

The natural coefficient sections are

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}(m)\in \Gamma(\mathcal F).
\]

The derived invariants worth testing are:

\[
\ell_r(m):=[A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}(m)]\in\mathbf P(\mathcal F_m),
\]

\[
D_{2r+1}(m)=\omega_h(A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}),
\]

\[
\mathcal E_r(m)=\|A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}(m)\|_{\wh}^2,
\]

and, along a path \(\Gamma:a\to b\),

\[
\Delta_{\Gamma}^{\mathrm{aff}}
=
\int_\Gamma U_{t\to b}E_{\eta\eta}(t)\,dt,
\]

where \(U_{t\to b}\) is parallel transport for \(\nabla^{\mathfrak f}\).

The obstruction row is

\[
E_{\eta\eta}
=
-\frac{\nu_{\eta\eta}}{24\pi q^3}(wI+uS)
\in \Gamma(\mathcal F).
\]

---

# 2. Projective invariant: useful but incomplete

The projective curve

\[
m\mapsto \ell_r(m)=[A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}(m)]
\]

detects changes of direction in the fixed-sector plane. The determinant

\[
D_{2r+1}
=
\omega_h(A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f})
\]

is exactly the projective incidence test:

\[
D_{2r+1}=0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
[A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}]=[A_5^{\mathfrak f}]
\]

when both vectors are nonzero.

Thus a relative winding candidate is

\[
\operatorname{Wind}_{r,5}(\Gamma)
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}
\int_\Gamma
d\log \omega_h(A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}).
\]

This counts projective crossings of \(\ell_r\) with \(\ell_5\), provided the path avoids zeros of the determinant except at controlled crossings.

But this invariant still misses pure line anomalies:

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}=\lambda_{2r+1}A_5^{\mathfrak f}.
\]

For these,

\[
\ell_{2r+1}=\ell_5,
\]

so the projective direction is entirely collapsed onto the reference line. The scale \(\lambda_{2r+1}\) may be nonzero and RH-relevant, but the projective invariant alone cannot distinguish

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}=0
\]

from

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}=\lambda A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\]

at the level of direction.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{projective/Maslov data are incomplete without scale or energy data.}
}
\]

A projective invariant can be a derived diagnostic of the rank-two architecture, but it cannot replace full vector transport.

---

# 3. Projective + scale package

A complete projective package would need

\[
\left(
[A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}],
\|A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}\|_{\wh}
\right),
\]

or equivalently

\[
\left(
D_{2r+1},
\lambda_{2r+1}
\right),
\]

or the full vector

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}.
\]

This restores detection of pure line anomalies. But once scale is restored, we are back to the split/full fixed-sector formulation. That means Bottleneck-C’s longitudinal defect returns unless it is globally transported by the rank-two connection.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{projective + scale is complete but not simpler than rank-two vector transport.}
}
\]

The projective part can help classify crossings and incidence, but it does not remove the need for a global affine transport theorem.

---

# 4. Energy invariant

The natural energy is

\[
\mathcal E_r
=
\|A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}\|_{\wh}^2.
\]

For \(r=3\),

\[
\mathcal E_7
=
\|A_7^{\mathfrak f}\|_{\wh}^2.
\]

Perturb

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}
\mapsto
A_7^{\mathfrak f}+\varepsilon E_{\eta\eta}.
\]

Then

\[
\mathcal E_7(\varepsilon)
=
\|A_7^{\mathfrak f}\|_{\wh}^2
+
2\operatorname{Re}
\left[
\varepsilon
\langle A_7^{\mathfrak f},E_{\eta\eta}\rangle_{\wh}
\right]
+
|\varepsilon|^2
\|E_{\eta\eta}\|_{\wh}^2.
\]

So the first variation is

\[
\delta\mathcal E_7
=
2\operatorname{Re}
\left[
\left\langle
A_7^{\mathfrak f},
-\frac{\nu_{\eta\eta}}{24\pi q^3}(wI+uS)
\right\rangle_{\wh}
\right].
\]

This has no fixed sign without a strong alignment theorem. The factor

\[
\nu_{\eta\eta}
\]

is itself sign-indefinite in the current framework, and the inner product

\[
\langle A_7^{\mathfrak f},wI+uS\rangle_{\wh}
\]

can also vary in phase/sign.

The second variation is

\[
\delta^2\mathcal E_7
=
2
\left|
\frac{\nu_{\eta\eta}}{24\pi q^3}
\right|^2
\|wI+uS\|_{\wh}^2
\ge0.
\]

This is positive, but tautological. It says only that a nonzero forcing vector has positive norm.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{energy detects the bad row but does not convert it into a contradiction.}
}
\]

To become a proof gate, one would need a global theorem forcing

\[
\mathcal E_r=0,
\]

or forcing a monotone boundary contradiction. No such theorem is currently present.

---

# 5. Affine displacement norm

If the rank-two affine transport equation has the form

\[
\nabla^{\mathfrak f}A=E,
\]

then along a path \(\Gamma:a\to b\),

\[
A(b)
=
U_\Gamma A(a)
+
\int_\Gamma U_{t\to b}E(t)\,dt.
\]

For the Bottleneck-C forcing,

\[
\Delta_\Gamma^{\eta\eta}
=
\int_\Gamma
U_{t\to b}
E_{\eta\eta}(t)\,dt.
\]

A possible scalar invariant is

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_{\Gamma}^{\mathrm{aff}}
=
\left\|
\Delta_\Gamma^{\eta\eta}
\right\|_{\wh,b}^2.
}
\]

This is gauge-covariant if the connection and metric are globally canonical.

But it requires all of the following:

1. a canonical path or cycle \(\Gamma\);
2. path-independent or controlled affine displacement;
3. endpoint/boundary conditions for \(A(a),A(b)\);
4. a theorem that off-line zeros force nonzero displacement;
5. a theorem that zeta/global symmetry forces zero displacement, or vice versa.

Without those, the displacement norm is just a measurement of accumulated forcing.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{affine displacement energy is promising only after Agent 4 supplies a global path/boundary theorem.}
}
\]

By itself, it is not a proof gate.

---

# 6. Does affine displacement have better sign than pointwise energy?

Not intrinsically.

Even if

\[
\mathcal E_{\Gamma}^{\mathrm{aff}}
=
\left\|
\int_\Gamma U_{t\to b}E_{\eta\eta}(t)\,dt
\right\|^2
\ge0,
\]

the integral can cancel:

\[
\int_\Gamma U_{t\to b}E_{\eta\eta}(t)\,dt=0
\]

despite nonzero pointwise forcing. Conversely, it can be nonzero without contradiction unless endpoints are constrained.

So the displacement norm has better coordinate behavior than pointwise \(\Phi_K\), but worse local decisiveness. It trades local obstruction for global control, and that control is currently missing.

---

# 7. Relation to projective/Maslov index

The projective winding

\[
\frac{1}{2\pi i}
\int_\Gamma d\log\omega_h(A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f})
\]

is only defined away from determinant zeros and measures directional crossings. It pairs naturally with the displacement/energy package:

\[
\left(
\operatorname{Wind}_{r,5}(\Gamma),
\left\|\Delta_\Gamma^{\mathrm{aff}}\right\|^2
\right).
\]

This pair detects both:

\[
\text{directional/projective motion}
\]

and

\[
\text{scale/line motion}.
\]

But it is not currently tied to RH. A proof would need something like:

\[
\text{off-line zero}\Rightarrow
\operatorname{Wind}_{r,5}\neq0
\quad\text{or}\quad
\Delta_\Gamma^{\mathrm{aff}}\neq0,
\]

and

\[
\text{global zeta symmetry}\Rightarrow
\operatorname{Wind}_{r,5}=0
\quad\text{and}\quad
\Delta_\Gamma^{\mathrm{aff}}=0.
\]

No such theorem is available from the current architecture.

---

# 8. Model showing energy remains non-decisive

Let the fixed-sector metric be standard and take a path where

\[
E_{\eta\eta}(t)=B
\]

on the first half of the path and

\[
E_{\eta\eta}(t)=-B
\]

on the second half, with trivial parallel transport.

Then pointwise energy is positive:

\[
\|E_{\eta\eta}(t)\|^2=\|B\|^2.
\]

But affine displacement is zero:

\[
\int_\Gamma E_{\eta\eta}(t)\,dt=0.
\]

So pointwise energy does not imply net displacement. Conversely, a nonzero displacement gives no contradiction without endpoint constraints.

This illustrates the gap:

\[
\boxed{
\text{energy needs boundary/monotonicity/index structure.}
}
\]

---

# 9. What would count as a positive result?

A derived projective/energy route would become viable if one could prove a theorem of the following form.

**Candidate theorem — Rank-two affine index-energy gate.**  
There exists a canonical path or cycle \(\Gamma\) and a rank-two connection \(\nabla^{\mathfrak f}\) such that:

1. the affine displacement

\[
\Delta_\Gamma^{\eta\eta}
=
\int_\Gamma U_{t\to b}E_{\eta\eta}(t)\,dt
\]

is globally well-defined;

2. off-line zeros force

\[
\Delta_\Gamma^{\eta\eta}\neq0
\]

or force nonzero projective winding;

3. global zeta/phase symmetry forces

\[
\Delta_\Gamma^{\eta\eta}=0
\]

and zero projective winding;

4. the contradiction does not require pointwise

\[
E_{\eta\eta}=0.
\]

This is a genuinely new global transport/index theorem. It is not currently in the proof.

---

# Final conclusion

\[
\boxed{
\text{Derived projective and energy invariants are useful diagnostics but not proof gates.}
}
\]

More specifically:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Projective/Maslov data are incomplete without scale.}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{Energy detects the obstruction but has sign-indefinite first variation and no zero-energy theorem.}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{Affine displacement energy may be meaningful only if a canonical global path/boundary theorem is proved.}
}
\]

Thus the derived invariants should be treated as secondary tools for the rank-two affine transport program, not as replacements for that program.

The next decisive theorem is not an energy lemma. It is the global affine transport/boundary theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can }E_{\eta\eta}\text{ be integrated as controlled rank-two affine forcing over canonical paths?}
}
\]

Until that theorem exists, the projective/energy route remains diagnostic, not proof-closing.
