## Assistant (2026-04-25 18:36:39)

_Thought for 11s_

# Agent 5 — Cocycle and Atlas Consistency Agent

## Verdict

The fixed-sector atlas theorem is **conditional, not proved**. For ordinary coordinate, endpoint-ordering, raw-frame, and whitening changes, the transition law can be written as a genuine

\[
G_{ij}\in GL_2(\mathbf C)
\]

transition, and the cocycle condition follows if the raw-frame and whitening choices are functorial. The unresolved and potentially fatal case is **source/core window transition**: moving zero atoms between core/source may change the corrected kernel state, not merely the coordinates of the same fixed-sector plane. If so, the rank-two bundle \(\mathcal F\) is only a local bookkeeping object, not a canonical global bundle. This is exactly the atlas issue isolated in the attached prompt. 

---

# 1. Candidate transition types

The intended bundle atlas is

\[
\mathcal F\to\mathcal B,
\qquad
\mathcal F_b=\mathfrak f_h(b)=\mathbf C I_b\oplus\mathbf C S_b.
\]

On overlap \(U_i\cap U_j\), we need

\[
(I_j,S_j)=(I_i,S_i)G_{ij},
\qquad
G_{ij}\in GL_2(\mathbf C),
\]

and on triple overlaps,

\[
G_{ij}G_{jk}=G_{ik}.
\]

The transition sources are:

1. midpoint chart transitions;
2. endpoint-ordering swaps;
3. raw jet-frame transitions;
4. whitening/Gram transitions;
5. source/core window transitions;
6. finite-core patch transitions.

I treat these separately.

---

# 2. Midpoint chart transitions

Let \(m_i\) and \(m_j\) be two local midpoint coordinates on the same good patch, with

\[
m_j=\varphi_{ij}(m_i),
\qquad
\varphi'_{ij}\neq0.
\]

If the fixed-sector frame is obtained by pushing forward the same ambient finite-\(s\) kernel state into two midpoint charts, then the frame change is linear:

\[
(I_j,S_j)=(I_i,S_i)G_{ij}^{m}.
\]

Here \(G_{ij}^m\) is determined by the Jacobian of the coordinate change and by how the odd/even jet coordinates scale.

On triple overlaps, ordinary chain rule gives

\[
G_{ij}^{m}G_{jk}^{m}=G_{ik}^{m}.
\]

So midpoint chart transitions are acceptable, provided the fixed-sector projection is functorial under the coordinate change.

**Status:** conditionally safe.

---

# 3. Endpoint-ordering swaps

Endpoint ordering swaps

\[
t_+\leftrightarrow t_-
\]

act on the natural fixed-sector basis by a finite matrix. In the simplest convention,

\[
I\mapsto I,
\qquad
S\mapsto -S
\]

or possibly

\[
S\mapsto S
\]

depending on whether \(S\) is defined as the symmetric or oriented swap coordinate. In either case,

\[
(I_j,S_j)=(I_i,S_i)G_{ij}^{\mathrm{swap}},
\]

with

\[
G_{ij}^{\mathrm{swap}}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
1&0\\
0&\pm1
\end{pmatrix}
\]

up to the draft’s exact sign convention.

Since swap squared is identity,

\[
(G_{ij}^{\mathrm{swap}})^2=I_2,
\]

the cocycle condition is harmless.

**Status:** safe if the endpoint orientation convention is fixed globally, or if swaps are explicitly included as transition maps.

---

# 4. Raw-frame and whitening transitions

Suppose the local fixed-sector frame is produced by whitening:

\[
E_i=(I_i,S_i)=E_i^{\rm raw}W_i.
\]

On overlap, assume raw frames satisfy

\[
E_j^{\rm raw}=E_i^{\rm raw}R_{ij},
\qquad
R_{ij}\in GL_2(\mathbf C).
\]

Then

\[
E_j=E_j^{\rm raw}W_j
=
E_i^{\rm raw}R_{ij}W_j
=
E_iW_i^{-1}R_{ij}W_j.
\]

Thus

\[
\boxed{
G_{ij}^{\rm wh}
=
W_i^{-1}R_{ij}W_j.
}
\]

So

\[
E_j=E_iG_{ij}^{\rm wh}.
\]

On triple overlaps,

\[
G_{ij}^{\rm wh}G_{jk}^{\rm wh}
=
W_i^{-1}R_{ij}W_j
W_j^{-1}R_{jk}W_k
=
W_i^{-1}R_{ij}R_{jk}W_k.
\]

If the raw-frame transitions satisfy

\[
R_{ij}R_{jk}=R_{ik},
\]

then

\[
G_{ij}^{\rm wh}G_{jk}^{\rm wh}=G_{ik}^{\rm wh}.
\]

So whitening transitions are valid \(GL_2\)-transitions if:

\[
W_i
\]

is chosen canonically on each good patch, for example by the principal positive square root of a positive Gram matrix, and raw-frame transitions are functorial.

The danger is not ordinary whitening. The danger is if different source/core conventions change the Gram object itself rather than merely its coordinate representation. Then \(W_i\) and \(W_j\) may not be related by the above formula.

**Status:** safe for fixed Gram object and canonical square root; conditional otherwise.

---

# 5. Finite-core patch transitions

If finite-core patch changes merely reparametrize the same finite-core configuration, then their effect is again linear on the fixed-sector frame:

\[
(I_j,S_j)=(I_i,S_i)G_{ij}^{\rm fc}.
\]

The cocycle condition follows from compatibility of the finite-core chart atlas.

But if a patch change changes which zeros are included in the core, then this becomes a source/core transition, not a mere finite-core coordinate transition. That case is not automatically safe.

**Status:** safe for coordinate changes within a fixed core; not safe for core-content changes.

---

# 6. Source/core window transitions: the sharp obstruction

This is the critical case.

Suppose two conventions differ by moving a zero atom \(\rho\) between source and core. Then the corrected kernel decomposition changes:

\[
q=q_{\core}+G_{\source}+B_{\bg}+E_{\est}
\]

becomes

\[
q=q_{\core}' +G_{\source}'+B_{\bg}+E_{\est}'.
\]

Even if the full \(q\) is unchanged, the **corrected local block**, the **affine-subtracted source**, and the **baseline/source tagging** may change.

The fixed-sector coefficients may transform as

\[
A_{2r+1,j}^{\mathfrak f}
=
G_{ij}^{-1}A_{2r+1,i}^{\mathfrak f}
\]

only if the move changes coordinates on the same corrected state.

But if moving \(\rho\) changes the corrected block by adding a source contribution, the transformation has the schematic form

\[
A_{2r+1,j}^{\mathfrak f}
=
G_{ij}^{-1}A_{2r+1,i}^{\mathfrak f}
+
B_{ij,\rho}^{(2r+1)},
\]

where

\[
B_{ij,\rho}^{(2r+1)}
\]

is an additive source/core correction.

That is **affine**, not linear. It does not define a rank-two vector-bundle transition.

If such additive terms occur, then

\[
(I_j,S_j)=(I_i,S_i)G_{ij}
\]

does not capture the transition of the actual fixed-sector data. The architecture would need an **affine bundle** or a bundle with connection and torsor transitions, not an ordinary rank-two vector bundle.

The deeper problem is path dependence. Moving atom \(\rho_1\) and then \(\rho_2\) may not yield the same intermediate corrected rows as moving \(\rho_2\) and then \(\rho_1\), unless the source/core bookkeeping has a strict additivity theorem. If not, the cocycle fails:

\[
G_{ij}G_{jk}\neq G_{ik}
\]

or worse, the transition is not even linear.

**Status:** unresolved and likely the main obstruction.

---

# 7. Affine transition detection

A transition is bad for the rank-two vector-bundle atlas if it has the form

\[
E_j=E_iG_{ij}+B_{ij}
\]

with

\[
B_{ij}\neq0.
\]

Then triple overlaps require

\[
B_{ik}=B_{ij}G_{jk}+B_{jk}.
\]

This is an affine cocycle condition. It defines a torsor/affine bundle, not a vector bundle.

That might still be useful for the later affine-transport architecture, but it is not the theorem currently being asked:

\[
\mathcal F_b=\mathfrak f_h(b)
\]

as a **rank-two vector bundle** with \(GL_2\)-transitions.

So the atlas wave has a fork:

\[
\boxed{
\text{linear transitions only}
\Rightarrow
\text{rank-two vector bundle survives;}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{affine source/core corrections}
\Rightarrow
\text{rank-two vector-bundle theorem fails; need affine bundle/torsor.}
}
\]

---

# 8. Can restrictions avoid the problem?

## Fixed global source/core convention

If the proof fixes one global source/core convention once and for all, then source/core transitions disappear.

Then the remaining transitions are midpoint, endpoint, raw-frame, whitening, and finite-core chart transitions. These are plausibly \(GL_2\)-linear and satisfy cocycle.

This gives a local-to-global object only over the restricted atlas:

\[
\mathcal B_{\rm fixed\ convention}.
\]

But this is canonical only if the chosen convention is canonical. A cutoff \(R\), moving core, or arbitrary window radius would not be acceptable unless fixed by a natural analytic rule.

## Fixed positive whitening branch

This is legitimate on a connected good patch where the Gram matrix stays positive definite and avoids branch crossing. It does not solve source/core noncanonicity.

## Fixed endpoint orientation

This is harmless. Endpoint swaps can also be treated as \(\mathbf Z_2\)-transition maps.

## Fixed finite-core patch type

This helps only if finite-core changes are coordinate refinements, not core-content changes.

---

# 9. Cocycle status by transition type

| Transition type | Linear \(GL_2\)? | Cocycle? | Status |
|---|---:|---:|---|
| Midpoint chart | Yes, if projection functorial | Yes by chain rule | Conditional safe |
| Endpoint swap | Yes | Yes, swap squared identity | Safe |
| Raw-frame change | Yes | Yes if raw atlas is valid | Conditional safe |
| Whitening change | Yes if same Gram object | Yes if \(W\) canonical | Conditional safe |
| Finite-core coordinate change | Yes if same core | Yes | Conditional safe |
| Source/core content change | Not necessarily; may be affine | Unproved, possibly path-dependent | Main obstruction |
| Moving cutoff/window | Not necessarily; may change corrected state | Unproved | Main obstruction |

---

# 10. Candidate theorem if the atlas is to survive

The necessary theorem is:

**Fixed-sector atlas theorem.**  
Fix a canonical global source/core convention and a good-patch atlas on which the Gram whitening is nondegenerate with principal branch. Then for every overlap \(U_i\cap U_j\), the fixed-sector frames satisfy

\[
(I_j,S_j)=(I_i,S_i)G_{ij},
\qquad
G_{ij}\in GL_2(\mathbf C),
\]

with

\[
G_{ij}G_{jk}=G_{ik}.
\]

Moreover, for every fixed-sector section \(A^{\mathfrak f}\),

\[
[A^{\mathfrak f}]_j=G_{ij}^{-1}[A^{\mathfrak f}]_i.
\]

This theorem is plausible only under a fixed convention. It does not follow if source/core window moves are treated as patch transitions.

---

# 11. If source/core transitions are unavoidable

If the proof needs to compare patches with different source/core windows, then the better object is probably not a vector bundle but an affine bundle:

\[
E_j=E_iG_{ij}+B_{ij}.
\]

Then the correct cocycle is

\[
G_{ij}G_{jk}=G_{ik},
\]

\[
B_{ik}=B_{ij}G_{jk}+B_{jk}.
\]

This may actually align with the “affine forcing” idea. The Bottleneck-C row

\[
E_{\eta\eta}
\]

would then be part of the affine displacement data.

But this is a different architecture:

\[
\boxed{
\text{rank-two affine fixed-sector torsor, not rank-two vector bundle.}
}
\]

It would require a new atlas theorem and a new connection theory.

---

# 12. Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The fixed-sector vector-bundle atlas is conditionally viable only under a fixed canonical source/core convention.}
}
\]

For ordinary coordinate, endpoint, raw-frame, and whitening transitions, the \(GL_2\)-cocycle works:

\[
G_{ij}=W_i^{-1}R_{ij}W_j,
\qquad
G_{ij}G_{jk}=G_{ik}.
\]

But the main unresolved issue is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{source/core window transitions may change the corrected state, not just the frame.}
}
\]

If they introduce additive source/core corrections, the transition is affine:

\[
E_j=E_iG_{ij}+B_{ij},
\]

not linear. Then the rank-two vector-bundle theorem fails as stated.

So the next decision is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Either fix a canonical global source/core convention and proceed with a vector bundle,}
}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{
\text{upgrade the architecture to a rank-two affine bundle/torsor with affine cocycles.}
}
\]

Without one of these, the rank-two route remains local and cannot support the global affine-transport theorem needed for the proof.
## User (2026-04-25 18:43:54)

Do your agent-specific instruction in the attached message.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 18:44:03)

_Thought for 4s_

# Agent 5 — Affine Atlas / Torsor Alternative Agent

## Verdict

A rank-two **affine torsor** is the right fallback if source/core transitions are not linear \(GL_2\)-frame changes. Moving atoms between source and core plausibly produces additive corrections

\[
A_j^{\mathfrak f}
=
G_{ij}^{-1}A_i^{\mathfrak f}
+
B_{ij},
\]

rather than a pure vector-bundle transition. That does **not** automatically kill the rank-two route, but it changes the architecture: the fixed-sector object becomes an affine bundle/torsor modeled on the rank-two bundle \(\mathcal F\), and the Bottleneck-C row becomes part of an affine displacement/forcing calculus rather than a vector-section defect. The attached wave explicitly identifies this as the alternative if source/core changes are affine rather than linear. 

The problem is that the affine cocycle is not yet proved. Without a cocycle theorem for the additive terms \(B_{ij}\), the source/core transition remains object-changing rather than functorial.

---

# 1. Linear atlas versus affine atlas

The desired rank-two vector-bundle transition is

\[
A_j^{\mathfrak f}
=
G_{ij}^{-1}A_i^{\mathfrak f},
\qquad
G_{ij}\in GL_2(\mathbf C).
\]

But source/core changes can alter the corrected local object. Then the more realistic transition is

\[
\boxed{
A_j^{\mathfrak f}
=
G_{ij}^{-1}A_i^{\mathfrak f}
+
B_{ij}.
}
\]

Here:

\[
B_{ij}\in \mathfrak f_{h,j}
\]

is an additive fixed-sector displacement caused by changing the core/source decomposition.

This is not a vector bundle. It is an affine bundle or torsor modeled on the vector bundle

\[
\mathcal F\to\mathcal B,
\qquad
\mathcal F_b=\mathfrak f_h(b).
\]

---

# 2. Where \(B_{ij}\) comes from

The relevant decomposition is schematically

\[
q
=
q_{\core}
+
q_{\aux,R}
+
T_{\far,R}
+
B_{\bg}
+
E_{\est,R}.
\]

A source/core transition changes one or more of these pieces. The most important case is moving a zero atom \(\rho\) into or out of the fixed core:

\[
q_{\core}^{(j)}
=
q_{\core}^{(i)}+q_\rho,
\]

\[
G_{\source}^{(j)}
=
G_{\source}^{(i)}-q_\rho.
\]

Even though the full phase object \(q\) is unchanged, the corrected local block, baseline/source labels, and residual source jets change. Thus the fixed-sector coefficients need not transform linearly.

For a fixed odd order,

\[
A_{2r+1,j}^{\mathfrak f}
-
G_{ij}^{-1}A_{2r+1,i}^{\mathfrak f}
\]

is the source/core transfer term. Denote it

\[
\boxed{
B_{ij,2r+1}
:=
A_{2r+1,j}^{\mathfrak f}
-
G_{ij}^{-1}A_{2r+1,i}^{\mathfrak f}.
}
\]

Thus the affine transition is

\[
\boxed{
A_{2r+1,j}^{\mathfrak f}
=
G_{ij}^{-1}A_{2r+1,i}^{\mathfrak f}
+
B_{ij,2r+1}.
}
\]

The full odd fixed-sector germ transition becomes

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal A_{\odd,j}^{\mathfrak f}
=
G_{ij}^{-1}\mathcal A_{\odd,i}^{\mathfrak f}
+
B_{ij,\odd}(s),
}
\]

where

\[
B_{ij,\odd}(s)
=
\sum_{r\ge0}B_{ij,2r+1}s^{2r+1}.
\]

---

# 3. Types of additive corrections

The additive term \(B_{ij}\) can have several sources.

## 3.1 Single-zero transfer contribution

Moving one zero atom \(\rho\) changes the local model by its phase contribution \(q_\rho\). The corresponding fixed-sector transfer is

\[
B_{ij,\rho}^{(2r+1)}
=
\pi_{\mathfrak f,j}
\left(
\Delta_\rho A_{2r+1}
\right),
\]

where

\[
\Delta_\rho A_{2r+1}
\]

is the change in the corrected coefficient after moving \(q_\rho\) between core and source.

This is the most structural \(B\)-term.

## 3.2 Cutoff-error transfer contribution

Changing \(R\) changes

\[
E_{\est,R}.
\]

Even if

\[
G^{\new}=q_{\aux,R}+T_{\far,R}
\]

is designed to be stable, the estimate/error bookkeeping may shift:

\[
E_{\est,R_j}-E_{\est,R_i}.
\]

The fixed-sector projection gives an additive correction

\[
B_{ij,E}^{(2r+1)}
=
\pi_{\mathfrak f,j}
\left(
\Delta_R E_{\est}^{(2r+1)}
\right).
\]

This is harmless only if \(E_{\est,R}\) is functorial and controlled.

## 3.3 Background transfer contribution

If \(B_{\bg}\) is reallocated or renormalized across patches, its fixed-sector odd contribution adds

\[
B_{ij,\bg}^{(2r+1)}.
\]

This should usually be avoided by fixing \(B_{\bg}\) globally.

## 3.4 Bottleneck-C row contribution

The bad row

\[
E_{\eta\eta}
=
-\frac{\nu_{\eta\eta}}{24\pi q^3}(wI+uS)
\]

is not itself necessarily a transition term. But in an affine architecture it is naturally the **infinitesimal affine displacement** associated to source deformation, i.e. a local version of \(B_{ij}\).

So Bottleneck C can be reinterpreted as:

\[
\boxed{
E_{\eta\eta}
\text{ is an infinitesimal affine forcing term in the torsor.}
}
\]

This is conceptually coherent. It does not yet prove anything.

---

# 4. Affine cocycle condition

Suppose transitions are

\[
A_j
=
G_{ij}^{-1}A_i+B_{ij}.
\]

Then on a triple overlap \(i,j,k\),

\[
A_k
=
G_{jk}^{-1}A_j+B_{jk}
=
G_{jk}^{-1}G_{ij}^{-1}A_i
+
G_{jk}^{-1}B_{ij}
+
B_{jk}.
\]

But also

\[
A_k
=
G_{ik}^{-1}A_i+B_{ik}.
\]

Therefore the linear parts must satisfy

\[
\boxed{
G_{ik}^{-1}
=
G_{jk}^{-1}G_{ij}^{-1},
}
\]

equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
G_{ik}=G_{ij}G_{jk},
}
\]

depending on convention.

The affine parts must satisfy

\[
\boxed{
B_{ik}
=
G_{jk}^{-1}B_{ij}+B_{jk}.
}
\]

This is the affine cocycle condition.

Thus the rank-two torsor theorem requires two statements:

\[
\boxed{
G_{ij}G_{jk}=G_{ik},
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
B_{ik}=G_{jk}^{-1}B_{ij}+B_{jk}.
}
\]

---

# 5. Is \(B_{ij}\) canonical?

This is the central issue.

For \(B_{ij}\) to define a torsor atlas, the additive correction must be determined only by the two conventions \(i,j\), not by the path or history of moving atoms.

For source/core changes, this requires an additivity theorem:

If one transfers atoms \(\rho_1,\rho_2\), then

\[
B_{\rho_1+\rho_2}
=
B_{\rho_1}
+
B_{\rho_2}
\]

after transporting to the same frame, and the result must be independent of order.

More precisely, if

\[
i\to j
\]

moves \(\rho_1\), and

\[
j\to k
\]

moves \(\rho_2\), then

\[
B_{ik}
=
G_{jk}^{-1}B_{ij}+B_{jk}.
\]

Failure of this condition means source/core transitions are **path-dependent**, hence not an affine atlas.

The danger is that whitening and correction are nonlinear in the core. Moving \(\rho_1\) first changes the Gram block and whitening before \(\rho_2\) is moved. Then the contribution of \(\rho_2\) may depend on the order. If so,

\[
B_{\rho_1,\rho_2}
\neq
B_{\rho_2,\rho_1}
\]

after alignment, and the affine cocycle fails.

So the unresolved theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{source/core transfer corrections are additive and order-independent after canonical transport.}
}
\]

I do not see this proved.

---

# 6. Does Gate A/Gate B control \(B_{ij}\)?

Only partially.

Gate A and Gate B are local algebraic cancellations under a fixed convention. They can show that certain homogeneous rows vanish or reduce once the source/core split is fixed.

They do not automatically imply that changing the split is functorial.

In particular, if \(B_{ij}\) includes a transfer of source jets into the core model, Gate A/B may ensure that some lower-grade terms remain closed, but the fixed-sector displacement

\[
B_{ij,2r+1}
\]

can still be nonzero.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Gate A/B do not by themselves prove affine cocycle functoriality.}
}
\]

They may help bound or simplify \(B_{ij}\), but they are not enough to identify source/core changes as coordinate changes.

---

# 7. Can Bottleneck-C be absorbed into the affine cocycle?

Conceptually, yes.

If the torsor atlas exists, then the Bottleneck-C forcing

\[
E_{\eta\eta}
\]

can be interpreted as an infinitesimal affine displacement. In a deformation parameter \(\tau\),

\[
\frac{D_{\mathrm{aff}}}{d\tau}A^{\mathfrak f}
=
E_{\eta\eta}+E_{\rm lower}.
\]

Then the obstruction no longer needs to vanish pointwise. Instead, one asks whether the accumulated affine displacement is globally constrained.

This would replace:

\[
E_{\eta\eta}=0
\]

by an affine transport law:

\[
A_j^{\mathfrak f}
=
G_{ij}^{-1}A_i^{\mathfrak f}
+
\int_i^j U_{t\to j}E_{\eta\eta}(t)\,dt
+
\text{lower terms}.
\]

But this requires:

1. affine atlas cocycle;
2. canonical affine connection;
3. global boundary/path/holonomy theorem;
4. compatibility with source/core transfer.

None is currently proved.

So the affine torsor route is plausible as a **language**, not yet as a proof.

---

# 8. If affine cocycle fails

If

\[
B_{ik}\neq G_{jk}^{-1}B_{ij}+B_{jk},
\]

then transitions depend on the path through conventions. That means source/core changes are not even affine coordinate changes.

Then the conclusion is severe:

\[
\boxed{
\text{source/core changes are object-changing, not functorial.}
}
\]

In that case, the only viable way to keep a global object is to fix a canonical source/core convention globally and forbid source/core transitions.

---

# 9. Two possible next architectures

## Option A — Fixed-convention vector bundle

Choose a canonical source/core convention once and for all.

Then there are no source/core transition terms, and the ordinary vector-bundle atlas may survive:

\[
A_j=G_{ij}^{-1}A_i.
\]

This is simpler, but depends on the existence of a genuinely canonical core rule.

## Option B — Affine torsor

Allow source/core changes, but upgrade transitions to

\[
A_j=G_{ij}^{-1}A_i+B_{ij}.
\]

This is more flexible and may naturally absorb \(E_{\eta\eta}\), but requires the affine cocycle theorem.

---

# 10. The affine torsor theorem needed

A precise theorem would be:

**Source/core affine functoriality theorem.**  
For every admissible source/core convention \(i\), let

\[
\mathcal A_i^{\mathfrak f}
\]

be the fixed-sector corrected odd germ. For every overlap \(i,j\), there exist canonical

\[
G_{ij}\in GL_2(\mathbf C)
\]

and

\[
B_{ij,\odd}(s)\in \mathfrak f_{h,j}[[s]]
\]

such that

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal A_j^{\mathfrak f}(s)
=
G_{ij}^{-1}\mathcal A_i^{\mathfrak f}(s)
+
B_{ij,\odd}(s).
}
\]

On triple overlaps,

\[
\boxed{
G_{ik}=G_{ij}G_{jk},
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
B_{ik,\odd}
=
G_{jk}^{-1}B_{ij,\odd}+B_{jk,\odd}.
}
\]

Moreover, the infinitesimal source/core transfer derivative of \(B\) has fixed-sector leading component

\[
\boxed{
dB_{\odd}
=
E_{\eta\eta}+E_{\rm lower}.
}
\]

If this theorem holds, the rank-two affine transport route can proceed.

If it fails, the global rank-two route fails unless a fixed canonical source/core convention is available.

---

# Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Source/core transitions are likely affine, not linear.}
}
\]

That means the ordinary rank-two vector-bundle atlas is probably too strict if source/core transitions are allowed.

The correct fallback is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a rank-two affine fixed-sector torsor modeled on }\mathcal F.
}
\]

But this is viable only if the affine corrections \(B_{ij}\) are canonical and satisfy the affine cocycle.

At present:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the affine torsor route is plausible but unproved.}
}
\]

The immediate next theorem is the source/core affine cocycle theorem:

\[
B_{ik}=G_{jk}^{-1}B_{ij}+B_{jk}.
\]

Without that, source/core transitions remain object-changing, and the rank-two route must either restrict to a fixed canonical core/source convention or be marked non-functorial.
## User (2026-04-25 18:52:01)

## Assistant (2026-04-25 18:52:17)

_Thought for 13s_

# Agent 5 — RH-Relevant Good-Patch Existence Agent

## Verdict

A reduced-base good-patch existence theorem is **plausible but conditional**. For a nondegenerate off-line zero configuration, one should be able to choose a midpoint \(m\) and a canonical symmetric same-scale core \(\mathcal C_k(m)\) satisfying strict gap, same-scale, endpoint, symmetry, and Gram nondegeneracy conditions. But the theorem is **not automatic**: configurations lying entirely on core-boundary strata, equal-distance/tie strata, symmetry-closure jumps, or Gram-singular strata are not covered unless the paper proves they are contained in a controlled exceptional set

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}.
\]

Thus the reduced-base rank-two route can proceed only with a theorem of the form:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Every RH-relevant nondegenerate off-line configuration admits }
b\in\mathcal B_{\rm red};
\text{ all failures lie in }\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}.
}
\]

This is exactly the existence question assigned to Agent 5 in the attached canonical-core wave. 

---

# 1. RH-relevant configuration

For this architecture, an RH-relevant configuration should mean at least:

\[
\rho=\beta+i\gamma,\qquad \beta\ne\frac12,
\]

together with its functional-equation and conjugation partners, producing a local off-line source/core anomaly visible to the fixed-sector odd germ.

A usable local patch additionally needs:

\[
m\ \text{near the relevant ordinate scale},
\]

a finite same-scale zero cluster

\[
\mathcal C_k(m),
\]

and good-patch hypotheses:

\[
d_k(m)<d_{k+1}(m),
\]

same-scale bounds,

\[
d_i(m)\asymp d_j(m)
\quad\text{for }i,j\le k,
\]

endpoint separation,

\[
s\neq0,\qquad t_+\neq t_-,
\]

symmetry closure under conjugation and functional equation,

\[
\rho\in\mathcal C_k(m)
\Rightarrow
\bar\rho,\ 1-\rho,\ 1-\bar\rho\in\mathcal C_k(m),
\]

noncollision / multiplicity control,

\[
\rho_i\neq \rho_j
\quad\text{or multiplicities explicitly handled},
\]

and Gram/whitening nondegeneracy,

\[
\det G_m\neq0.
\]

Only under these conditions is the reduced-base fixed-sector bundle

\[
\mathcal F_{\rm red}\to\mathcal B_{\rm red}
\]

well-defined.

---

# 2. Candidate good-patch construction

Given a simple off-line zero

\[
\rho=\beta+i\gamma,
\qquad \beta\neq\frac12,
\]

the natural local center is the midpoint scale associated to its ordinate, i.e. the \(m\)-value for which the source-pair distance

\[
x=2m-\gamma
\]

is small. Formally, one chooses \(m\) in a small interval around

\[
m_0=\gamma/2
\]

or the corresponding draft midpoint convention.

Then define \(\mathcal C_k(m)\) as the canonical symmetric same-scale cluster: the smallest or fixed-size cluster containing the relevant off-line quadruple and closed under the symmetries

\[
\rho\mapsto\bar\rho,\qquad
\rho\mapsto1-\rho.
\]

The core is stable if there is a strict gap between the last selected core atom and the first non-core atom:

\[
d_k(m)<d_{k+1}(m).
\]

Since the zero set is discrete, strict inequalities are open conditions. Therefore, if such a strict gap holds at one point \(m\), it holds in a small neighborhood.

So the local existence mechanism is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{simple off-line zero}
+
\text{strict core gap}
+
\text{Gram nondegeneracy}
\Rightarrow
b\in\mathcal B_{\rm red}.
}
\]

This is the positive part.

---

# 3. Failure modes

The reduced-base theorem fails or becomes conditional at the following strata.

## 3.1 Nearest-neighbor tie

If

\[
d_k(m)=d_{k+1}(m),
\]

then the \(k\)-nearest core is not locally stable. A tiny change in \(m\) can change core membership.

This is a core-boundary stratum:

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm tie}^{\core}
=
\{m:d_k(m)=d_{k+1}(m)\}.
\]

## 3.2 Equal-spacing degeneracy

If several zero distances coincide because of local symmetry or spacing degeneracy, the same issue occurs. The canonical core may jump unless the tie is resolved by a canonical symmetry-closed rule.

This belongs in

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm spacing}^{\core}.
\]

## 3.3 Symmetry-closure jump

The nearest \(k\) atoms may not be closed under

\[
\rho\mapsto\bar\rho,
\qquad
\rho\mapsto1-\rho.
\]

Enforcing symmetry closure can force the core size to jump:

\[
k\mapsto k+2,\ k+4,\ldots
\]

If that jump is not stable, the patch is not in the reduced base.

This defines

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm sym}^{\core}.
\]

## 3.4 Endpoint-gap failure

If the local endpoint separation collapses or leaves the allowed microscopic scale, the finite-\(s\) construction is not valid:

\[
s=0
\quad\text{or}\quad
s\notin\text{allowed scale}.
\]

This is already a geometric exceptional condition, but it should be included in

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}.
\]

## 3.5 Gram/whitening singularity

If

\[
\det G_m=0
\]

or the principal whitening branch fails, then the fixed-sector frame and connection cannot be defined canonically.

Include

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm Gram}^{\core}
=
\{m:\det G_m=0\}.
\]

## 3.6 Zero collision / multiplicity

Multiple zeros or coincident local ordinates can make core selection ambiguous unless multiplicities are explicitly built into the convention.

This belongs in

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm coll}^{\core}.
\]

---

# 4. Exceptional set

The right enlarged exceptional set is:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}
=
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm tie}^{\core}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm spacing}^{\core}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm sym}^{\core}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm Gram}^{\core}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm coll}^{\core}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm endpoint}^{\core}.
}
\]

The required containment is:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal B\setminus\mathcal B_{\rm red}
\subseteq
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}.
}
\]

This is plausible if each failure condition is given by explicit analytic equalities/inequalities in the local zero positions and Gram entries.

But this containment must be proved. It cannot be assumed.

---

# 5. Does \(\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}\) become too large?

This is the main risk.

If every off-line zero configuration could be forced into

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core},
\]

then the reduced-base strategy would not detect RH violations.

The proof needs a nontrivial existence theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{For every RH-relevant off-line zero configuration, either}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{there exists }b\in\mathcal B_{\rm red},
}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{
\text{the configuration lies in an already-controlled exceptional case.}
}
\]

The second clause is dangerous. “Exceptional” cannot merely mean “not handled by the proof.” It must mean one of:

\[
\text{impossible by known zero geometry},
\]

\[
\text{already contradictory by a separate local theorem},
\]

or

\[
\text{included in a finite-core incidence set whose contribution is controlled}.
\]

At present, this is not established.

---

# 6. Conditional existence theorem

The theorem one can currently state is conditional.

**Proposition — Conditional reduced-good-patch existence.**  
Let \(\rho=\beta+i\gamma\), \(\beta\ne1/2\), be a simple off-critical zero whose symmetry-closed same-scale cluster near the relevant midpoint \(m_0\) satisfies:

\[
d_k(m_0)<d_{k+1}(m_0),
\]

endpoint separation,

\[
s(m_0)\neq0,
\]

same-scale bounds,

\[
d_i(m_0)\asymp d_j(m_0)\quad(i,j\le k),
\]

symmetry closure,

\[
\mathcal C_k(m_0)=\overline{\mathcal C_k(m_0)}
=
1-\mathcal C_k(m_0),
\]

and Gram nondegeneracy,

\[
\det G_{m_0}\neq0.
\]

Then there is a neighborhood \(U\ni m_0\) such that, for all \(m\in U\), the canonical core

\[
\mathcal C_k(m)
\]

is locally stable as a labeled symmetric same-scale cluster. Hence

\[
m\in\mathcal B_{\rm red}.
\]

This gives local existence for nondegenerate RH-relevant configurations.

---

# 7. Missing theorem

The missing stronger theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Every RH-relevant off-line zero configuration is either nondegenerate as above or lies in a controlled exceptional set.}
}
\]

Formally:

\[
\boxed{
\text{RH-relevant off-line configuration}
\Rightarrow
\exists b\in\mathcal B_{\rm red}
\quad\text{or}\quad
b\in\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}\text{ with controlled incidence.}
}
\]

This theorem is not currently proved.

---

# 8. Possible no-go scenarios

The reduced-base strategy would fail if one of the following is possible.

## Scenario A — all candidate midpoints are boundary points

For an off-line configuration, every natural midpoint \(m\) satisfies

\[
d_k(m)=d_{k+1}(m)
\]

or a symmetry-closure ambiguity. Then no stable canonical core exists.

## Scenario B — symmetry closure forces unstable core size

If including functional-equation/conjugation partners always forces atoms across the \(k/k+1\) boundary, the core rule jumps under arbitrarily small perturbation.

## Scenario C — Gram singularity unavoidable

If the fixed-sector Gram block is singular at all RH-relevant midpoints for a certain off-line configuration, then no whitened fixed-sector bundle exists there.

## Scenario D — exceptional set swallows RH-relevant cases

If

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}
\]

contains the entire RH-relevant off-line locus, then the reduced-base proof has no reach.

None of these is shown to occur, but none is excluded by the current ledger.

---

# 9. Impact on rank-two route

The rank-two route may proceed only under the following conditional statement:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Work on }\mathcal B_{\rm red}
\text{ and separately prove RH-relevant good-patch existence.}
}
\]

That is, the next architecture can be developed locally on

\[
\mathcal B_{\rm red},
\]

but the global proof must later prove that an off-line zero gives at least one reduced good patch.

Without that existence theorem, the rank-two fixed-sector bundle may be perfectly valid but irrelevant to RH.

---

# Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Reduced good-patch existence is plausible for nondegenerate off-line configurations.}
}
\]

But:

\[
\boxed{
\text{It is not yet proved for all RH-relevant configurations.}
}
\]

The exact dependency is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{RH-relevant off-line zero}
\Rightarrow
\exists b\in\mathcal B_{\rm red}
\quad
\text{or controlled exceptional incidence.}
}
\]

If this theorem is proved, the rank-two route can proceed on the reduced fixed-core base. If it fails, the rank-two route is not globally applicable, even if the local fixed-sector bundle is well-defined.

# Agent 5 — Exceptional-Set Control and Non-Swallowing Agent

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}
\text{ is structurally controllable as a union of analytic/semianalytic boundary strata,}
}
\]

but the strong non-swallowing theorem

\[
\boxed{
\forall\ \text{RH-relevant configuration},\quad
\exists b\in\mathcal B_{\rm red}
}
\]

is **not yet proved**. The reasonable current statement is conditional:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Every nondegenerate RH-relevant packet admits nearby reduced good patches, unless it lies entirely in a controlled core-boundary/incidence stratum.}
}
\]

The missing point is to prove that the enlarged exceptional set does **not** contain all RH-relevant off-line configurations. The attached coordinator prompt correctly identifies this as one of the kill switches for the reduced-base rank-two route. 

---

# 1. Exceptional-set decomposition

The enlarged exceptional set should be written as

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}
=
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm tie}^{\core}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm scale}^{\core}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm sym}^{\core}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm endpoint}^{\core}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm Gram}^{\core}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm patch}^{\core}.
\]

A more explicit ledger is:

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm tie}^{\core}
=
\{d_k=d_{k+1}\},
\]

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm scale}^{\core}
=
\{\text{same-scale inequalities fail}\},
\]

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm sym}^{\core}
=
\{\text{symmetry closure changes the selected core size or labels}\},
\]

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm endpoint}^{\core}
=
\{\text{endpoint separation/collision/microscopic scale fails}\},
\]

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm Gram}^{\core}
=
\{\det G_{\core}=0\ \text{or whitening branch degenerates}\},
\]

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm patch}^{\core}
=
\{\text{chart boundary, label boundary, or finite-core patch transition}\}.
\]

---

# 2. Classification by type

| Component | Type | Expected codimension | Controlled? |
|---|---:|---:|---|
| \(\mathcal E_{\rm tie}^{\core}\) | analytic equality \(d_k-d_{k+1}=0\) | codim \(\ge1\), unless identically tied | yes, if ties are not forced |
| \(\mathcal E_{\rm scale}^{\core}\) | semianalytic inequality boundary | codim \(\ge1\) boundary | yes, but depends on chosen scale constants |
| \(\mathcal E_{\rm sym}^{\core}\) | symmetry-closure boundary / label jump | codim \(\ge1\) generically | conditional |
| \(\mathcal E_{\rm endpoint}^{\core}\) | endpoint collision or scale boundary | codim \(\ge1\) | yes, if endpoints are freely choosable |
| \(\mathcal E_{\rm Gram}^{\core}\) | analytic determinant vanishing | codim \(\ge1\), unless determinant identically zero | conditional |
| \(\mathcal E_{\rm patch}^{\core}\) | chart / label boundary | semianalytic boundary | mostly bookkeeping |
| Existing \(\mathcal E_{\rm geom}\) | preexisting incidence/geometry exceptional set | by prior definitions | assumed controlled only if old theorem holds |

The dangerous cases are those where a condition is not merely a boundary but is **identically forced** on the RH-relevant locus. For example:

\[
d_k(m)\equiv d_{k+1}(m)
\]

on the entire relevant family would make \(\mathcal E_{\rm tie}^{\core}\) swallow that family. Likewise,

\[
\det G_{\core}(m)\equiv0
\]

on an off-line packet family would kill the reduced base there.

---

# 3. Codimension and non-swallowing

For a fixed finite zero packet with distinct local ordinates, the tie condition

\[
d_\alpha(m)=d_\beta(m)
\]

is an analytic equation in \(m\) and the packet parameters. Unless the two distance functions are identically equal by symmetry, this is a codimension-one wall. Therefore a strict core gap

\[
d_k(m)<d_{k+1}(m)
\]

should hold on open chambers between tie walls.

Similarly, Gram degeneracy

\[
\det G_{\core}(m)=0
\]

is analytic. Unless the Gram determinant vanishes identically for structural reasons, its complement is open and dense in the local parameter space.

Thus the natural non-swallowing statement is:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}
\text{ has empty interior inside the nondegenerate RH-relevant packet space.}
}
\]

But this is still a theorem to prove. It requires showing that none of the exceptional equalities is identically forced by the off-line zero symmetries.

---

# 4. Can \(\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}\) contain an open RH-relevant set?

There are three real risks.

## Risk A — symmetry-forced ties

The functional-equation/conjugation packet may force equal distances from a natural midpoint. Some equalities are expected. The core rule must absorb these by selecting the whole symmetry-closed packet, not by treating the forced equality as a boundary.

The correct approach is:

\[
\text{distance ties within a required symmetry packet are allowed;}
\]

\[
\text{ties between the selected packet and outside atoms are exceptional.}
\]

So \(\mathcal E_{\rm tie}^{\core}\) should use packet distances, not individual zero distances.

## Risk B — symmetry closure forces core jumps

If selecting a nearest atom forces inclusion of its partners, the core size may jump. This is manageable only if \(k\) is defined as the size of a symmetry-closed packet class, not as a naive count of individual atoms.

So the reduced core should be:

\[
\mathcal C_k(m)
=
\text{the nearest }k\text{ symmetry packets, expanded to all partners}.
\]

rather than “nearest \(k\) individual zeros.”

## Risk C — Gram degeneracy on the relevant packet

If the fixed-sector Gram block becomes singular precisely on off-line symmetric packets, then \(\mathcal E_{\rm Gram}^{\core}\) could swallow RH-relevant cases. I do not see a reason this must happen, but it must be ruled out by a determinant nonvanishing lemma or by showing singular cases are already controlled incidence.

---

# 5. Compatibility with existing incidence methods

Some components can likely be absorbed into existing finite-core incidence logic:

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm Gram}^{\core},
\quad
\mathcal E_{\rm endpoint}^{\core},
\quad
\mathcal E_{\rm patch}^{\core}
\]

are natural geometric degeneracy sets.

But

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm tie}^{\core},
\quad
\mathcal E_{\rm sym}^{\core},
\quad
\mathcal E_{\rm scale}^{\core}
\]

need explicit addition to the incidence ledger. They are not automatically part of the old exceptional set unless the paper defines them.

The required containment is:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}
\subseteq
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\rm old}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm core\ boundary}
}
\]

with

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm core\ boundary}
\]

proved controlled by analytic boundary/incidence arguments.

---

# 6. Non-swallowing theorem needed

The exact theorem should be:

**Theorem — Core exceptional non-swallowing.**  
Let \(\mathcal R_{\rm RH}\) be the space of RH-relevant off-line zero packets satisfying the minimal noncollision and symmetry assumptions. Then either:

\[
\mathcal R_{\rm RH}\cap \mathcal B_{\rm red}\neq\varnothing
\]

for every packet family, or any packet family contained in

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}
\]

belongs to a separately controlled incidence class.

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm RH}
\not\subseteq
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}
}
\]

unless the entire RH-relevant configuration is already handled by a different contradiction or incidence theorem.

At present, this theorem is **not proved**.

---

# 7. Stronger target

The stronger target from the prompt is:

\[
\boxed{
\forall\ \text{RH-relevant configuration},\quad
\exists\ \text{nearby } b\in\mathcal B_{\rm red}.
}
\]

This is stronger than mere non-swallowing. It requires perturbability of the local midpoint/sample choice while keeping the same RH-relevant packet visible.

A plausible conditional version is:

**Proposition — Conditional nearby reduced patch.**  
If an RH-relevant packet is not contained in a symmetry-forced core-boundary stratum and if its Gram determinant is not identically zero, then there exists a nearby midpoint/sample choice

\[
b\in\mathcal B_{\rm red}.
\]

Reason: the excluded conditions are finitely many analytic equalities and semianalytic boundaries. Their complement is open and nonempty if they are not identically satisfied.

This is the theorem I would try to prove next.

---

# 8. Final status

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}
\text{ is a reasonable controlled exceptional set candidate.}
}
\]

But:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The non-swallowing theorem is still missing.}
}
\]

The rank-two reduced-base route remains viable only under the dependency:

\[
\boxed{
\text{RH-relevant off-line packets can be represented away from }
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}.
}
\]

If this fails, the reduced-base rank-two route has no global reach. If it succeeds, the next theorem is the canonical connection construction on

\[
\mathcal F_{\rm red}\to\mathcal B_{\rm red}.
\]

# Agent 5 — Fixed-Sector Visibility Non-Swallowing Agent

## Verdict

Fixed-sector visibility is **plausible but not proved**. A genuine off-line packet should generically produce nonzero odd fixed-sector data

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}
=
(A_7^{\mathfrak f},A_9^{\mathfrak f},\ldots,A_{2R+1}^{\mathfrak f}),
\]

but the current framework still needs a theorem excluding the possibility that all RH-relevant off-line packets lie in the fixed-sector invisibility locus

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}
=
\{C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}=0\}.
\]

The attached instruction makes this one of the required pieces of the Exceptional Non-Swallowing Theorem: reduced good patches are useful only if the off-line packet is actually visible to the rank-two fixed-sector germ. 

---

# 1. Fixed-sector finite vector

The relevant vector should be

\[
\boxed{
C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}(m,s)
=
(A_7^{\mathfrak f},A_9^{\mathfrak f},\ldots,A_{2R_{\Theta_k}+1}^{\mathfrak f})(m,s).
}
\]

Here

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}
\in
\mathfrak f_h
=
\mathbf C I\oplus \mathbf C S.
\]

The fixed-sector visibility condition is

\[
\boxed{
C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}(m,s)\neq0.
}
\]

Equivalently, at least one controlled odd coefficient satisfies

\[
\boxed{
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}(m,s)\neq0.
}
\]

The invisibility locus is

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}
:=
\left\{
(m,s,\mathcal C_k):
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}=0
\text{ for all }7\le 2r+1\le 2R_{\Theta_k}+1
\right\}.
}
\]

This should be added to the enlarged exceptional set unless a visibility theorem proves it cannot contain RH-relevant packets.

---

# 2. What off-line data enter the coefficients?

An off-line packet is

\[
\mathcal P(\rho)
=
\{\rho,\bar\rho,1-\rho,1-\bar\rho\},
\qquad
\rho=\beta+i\gamma,
\qquad
\beta\neq\frac12.
\]

Its phase-source contribution contains the pair atom

\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)
=
\frac{1-\beta}{(1-\beta)^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}
+
\frac{\beta}{\beta^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}.
\]

After affine subtraction at \(m\), the packet contributes higher jets

\[
\rho_2,\rho_4,\rho_6,\ldots
\]

depending analytically and nontrivially on

\[
\beta,\gamma,m.
\]

Earlier Zeta-side calculations showed that these jets are not locked to the Bottleneck-C cancellation identity and that far exterior actual source atoms approximate the bad formal germ \(r=u^2\). That is strong evidence that actual off-line packet contributions are not automatically invisible to the fixed-sector algebra.

But evidence is not enough: the current draft still needs a theorem that some finite fixed-sector coefficient is nonzero for an off-line packet outside controlled incidence.

---

# 3. Can symmetry force complete invisibility?

Symmetry can force some components to vanish. That is not surprising.

The dangerous claim would be stronger:

\[
\boxed{
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}=0
\quad
\forall r\le R_{\Theta_k}
}
\]

for every RH-relevant off-line symmetry packet.

I do not see a structural reason this should be true.

The packet

\[
\{\rho,\bar\rho,1-\rho,1-\bar\rho\}
\]

is symmetric globally, but off-line displacement

\[
\beta-\frac12\neq0
\]

is precisely a symmetry-breaking parameter relative to the critical line. Analytic dependence on this parameter should generically create nonzero fixed-sector odd coefficients unless the fixed-sector projection kills the entire off-line direction.

Thus the expected picture is:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}
\text{ is an analytic incidence locus, not the full RH-relevant packet space.}
}
\]

But this must be proved by showing at least one coefficient function

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}(\beta,\gamma,m,s)
\]

is not identically zero on the off-line packet family.

---

# 4. Relation to prior obstruction computations

The prior local obstruction computations strongly suggest visibility.

The Bottleneck-C row has the form

\[
E_{\eta\eta}
=
-\frac{\nu_{\eta\eta}}{24\pi q^3}(wI+uS).
\]

For formal or far-tail actual source models, one obtains

\[
\nu_{\eta\eta}\neq0
\]

under simple good-patch assumptions such as

\[
q_0\neq0,
\qquad
q_0''=0,
\qquad
A_2\neq0.
\]

Then

\[
E_{\eta\eta}\neq0
\]

provided

\[
wI+uS\neq0.
\]

This shows that the fixed-sector machinery can detect the off-line-like source shape. It does **not** by itself prove that every actual off-line core packet produces nonzero finite vector

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}.
\]

So the correct use of the prior obstruction is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{fixed-sector invisibility is not forced by the algebra;}
}
\]

not:

\[
\boxed{
\text{fixed-sector visibility is already proved for all RH-relevant packets.}
}
\]

---

# 5. Analytic-incidence formulation

On a reduced good patch, each coefficient

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}
\]

is analytic in the local packet/sample parameters.

Therefore

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}
=
\bigcap_{r=3}^{R_{\Theta_k}}
\{A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}=0\}
\]

is an analytic or semianalytic incidence set.

The needed non-swallowing theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm RH}
\not\subseteq
\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}.
}
\]

A stronger and more useful version is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{For every nondegenerate RH-relevant packet, there exists }(m,s)
\text{ near the packet such that }
C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}(m,s)\neq0.
}
\]

To prove it, it is enough to show that for each RH-relevant packet family, at least one coefficient function

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}
\]

is not identically zero as a function of \((m,s)\).

---

# 6. Possible countermodel / failure mode

The route would fail if there exists an open family of off-line packets such that

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}(m,s)=0
\]

for all controlled \(r\) and all admissible nearby \((m,s)\).

That would mean the off-line packet is invisible to the rank-two odd fixed-sector germ.

Possible mechanisms:

1. **Projection annihilation:**  
   The fixed-sector projection

   \[
   \pi_{\mathfrak f}
   \]

   kills the entire off-line packet contribution.

2. **Symmetry cancellation:**  
   The functional-equation/conjugation packet cancels all odd fixed-sector terms.

3. **Finite-depth miss:**  
   Nonzero signal exists only beyond

   \[
   2R_{\Theta_k}+1.
   \]

4. **Core normalization absorption:**  
   The selected core absorbs the off-line contribution into baseline data so that the corrected odd residual vanishes through finite order.

None of these has been proved possible, but none has been excluded in the current ledger.

---

# 7. Required theorem

The theorem needed is:

**Fixed-sector visibility theorem.**  
Let \(\mathcal P(\rho)\), \(\beta\ne1/2\), be a nondegenerate RH-relevant off-line packet. Suppose there exists a reduced good patch

\[
b=(m,s,\mathcal C_k(m))\in\mathcal B_{\rm red}
\]

with the packet contained in the canonical core and satisfying strict gap, same-scale, symmetry-closure, endpoint, and Gram nondegeneracy assumptions. Then

\[
\boxed{
C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}(b)\neq0
}
\]

unless

\[
b\in \mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core},
\]

where

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}
\]

is a controlled analytic incidence set of codimension at least one.

The stronger form required for the global proof is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No RH-relevant off-line packet family is contained entirely in }
\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}.
}
\]

---

# 8. Where this leaves the non-swallowing theorem

The full reduced good-patch existence theorem now has one more explicit condition:

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}(m,s)\neq0.
\]

So the target becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\forall \mathcal P(\rho)\in\mathcal R_{\rm RH},
\quad
\exists(m,s)
\text{ such that}
}
\]

\[
d_k(m)<d_{k+1}(m),
\]

same-scale holds,

symmetry closure is stable,

endpoint separation holds,

\[
\det G_{\core}(m,s)\neq0,
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}(m,s)\neq0.
}
\]

If the last condition fails only on controlled analytic incidence, then the rank-two reduced-base route survives.

If the last condition fails identically for a class of off-line packets, the route has no RH-detection power there.

---

# Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Fixed-sector visibility is a necessary new dependency.}
}
\]

It is plausible because off-line packet jets depend nontrivially on

\[
\beta-\frac12,
\]

and prior obstruction computations show that actual source shapes can produce nonzero fixed-sector rows.

But the proof is missing. The current correct status is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Visibility non-swallowing is conditional on proving }
\mathcal R_{\rm RH}\not\subseteq\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}.
}
\]

The enlarged exceptional set should therefore become

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core,+}
=
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}.
}
\]

The next step is to prove that this enlarged set still does not swallow RH-relevant off-line packets.

# Agent 5 — Adversarial Visibility Countermodel Agent

## Verdict

I do **not** find a clean adversarial countermodel showing that a genuine off-line packet can satisfy

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}\equiv0
\]

on an open nondegenerate RH-relevant family outside the controlled exceptional set. The strongest adversarial attempts either collapse to the critical-line case, rely on special midpoint/sample parity, or define exactly the invisibility incidence locus

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}.
\]

So the visibility theorem survives this adversarial pass, but it is **not proved**. The remaining proof obligation is still to show that

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}
=
\{C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}=0\}
\]

is a proper analytic incidence set, not an open RH-relevant family. The attached prompt correctly assigns this as the “try to break visibility” task. 

---

# 1. Formal packet model

Take an off-line packet

\[
\mathcal P(\rho)=\{\rho,\bar\rho,1-\rho,1-\bar\rho\},
\qquad
\rho=\beta+i\gamma,
\qquad
\beta\ne\frac12.
\]

The paired source atom is

\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)
=
\frac{1-\beta}{(1-\beta)^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}
+
\frac{\beta}{\beta^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}.
\]

Set

\[
\delta=\beta-\frac12,
\qquad
a=1-\beta=\frac12-\delta,
\qquad
b=\beta=\frac12+\delta,
\qquad
x=2t-\gamma.
\]

Then

\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)
=
\frac{a}{a^2+x^2}
+
\frac{b}{b^2+x^2}.
\]

This expression is symmetric under

\[
\delta\mapsto-\delta,
\]

so the off-line displacement enters through even powers of \(\delta\). That creates a possible weak point: a detector linear in \(\delta\) would miss the packet. But a rank-two fixed-sector detector is not known to be linear in \(\delta\), and previous source-jet calculations show the actual packet changes nontrivially with \(\delta^2\). Thus symmetry does not by itself annihilate the packet.

---

# 2. Midpoint parity attack

Choose the special midpoint

\[
m=\gamma/2,
\]

so

\[
x_m=2m-\gamma=0.
\]

Then

\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(m+u)
=
\frac{a}{a^2+4u^2}
+
\frac{b}{b^2+4u^2}
\]

is an even function of \(u\). Therefore all odd derivatives of the raw packet atom vanish at \(u=0\).

This is the strongest local parity attack.

However, it does **not** produce a decisive countermodel. The Bottleneck-C obstruction already arises from even source jets such as

\[
\rho_2,\rho_4,
\]

feeding an odd fixed-sector row after the finite-\(s\), whitening, and mixed-block algebra. In particular, the known bad row is precisely an \(\eta\eta\) second-source effect, not a raw odd derivative of the source atom.

So the midpoint-evenness attack only proves:

\[
\text{raw odd source derivatives can vanish at a symmetric midpoint.}
\]

It does **not** prove:

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}=0
\quad\forall r.
\]

To become a real countermodel, one would need to show that the whole fixed-sector odd germ depends only on raw odd derivatives of \(f_{\beta,\gamma}\). The current Bottleneck-C algebra shows the opposite: even source jets can generate odd-order fixed-sector effects.

---

# 3. Critical-line collapse attack

If

\[
\delta=0,
\qquad
\beta=\frac12,
\]

then the packet lies on the critical line. Many off-line-sensitive expressions can vanish there.

But this is not an RH-relevant off-line countermodel. It belongs to the boundary

\[
\beta-\frac12=0.
\]

So any invisibility that depends on

\[
\delta=0
\]

is irrelevant to the requested theorem, which assumes

\[
\delta\ne0.
\]

A valid adversarial model must keep \(\delta\ne0\) on an open family.

I do not find such a model from critical-line symmetry alone.

---

# 4. Endpoint/sample cancellation attack

One could try to choose the sample separation \(s\) so that the finite-\(s\) endpoint contributions cancel:

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}(m,s)=0
\]

for several controlled orders.

This can occur at isolated sample values. But to defeat visibility, it must occur for all admissible nearby \((m,s)\), or at least for every \((m,s)\) in the reduced good-patch neighborhood.

That would require the analytic functions

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}(m,s)
\]

to vanish identically in \((m,s)\). Isolated cancellations are just incidence:

\[
(m,s)\in\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}.
\]

Thus endpoint/sample tuning gives at most a controlled exceptional set, not an open-family countermodel.

---

# 5. Anti-fixed-sector attack

Try to force the entire off-line packet contribution into the anti-fixed complement:

\[
A_{2r+1}
\in
\ker\pi_{\mathfrak f}
\quad
\forall r.
\]

This would imply

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}=0
\quad
\forall r.
\]

This is the most dangerous conceptual countermodel.

However, I do not find a structural reason functional-equation/conjugation symmetry forces off-line packets into the anti-fixed sector. The known obstruction row has the explicit fixed-sector shape

\[
E_{\eta\eta}
=
-\frac{\nu_{\eta\eta}}{24\pi q^3}(wI+uS),
\]

which lies in

\[
\mathfrak f_h.
\]

So the same general source calculus already produces fixed-sector contributions from source packets. To maintain the anti-fixed countermodel, one would need to prove that **actual core off-line packets** differ from the tested source packets in a way that lands entirely in \(\ker\pi_{\mathfrak f}\). There is no current evidence for that.

Thus the anti-fixed-sector attack fails unless a new symmetry identity is supplied.

---

# 6. Core-normalization absorption attack

Another possibility is that when the off-line packet is included in

\[
q_{\core},
\]

the core normalization absorbs its contribution into baseline/reference data, leaving the corrected odd residual invisible:

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}=0.
\]

This is not impossible. It is the most serious remaining failure mode.

But if this happens, it is exactly the analytic invisibility incidence condition:

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}
=
\{C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}=0\}.
\]

To become a true counterexample, one must show this absorption happens identically for an open off-line packet family. I do not see that.

Moreover, prior formal and actual-source tests show that off-line-like source data can create nonzero fixed-sector rows. That makes universal absorption implausible, though still unproved.

So the result is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{core absorption is a live incidence risk, not an explicit open-family countermodel.}
}
\]

---

# 7. Far-tail actual-source adversarial test

For far exterior actual source atoms, previous calculations gave source jets approximating the formal bad germ

\[
r(u)=u^2.
\]

That model produces nonzero Bottleneck-C fixed-sector obstruction under good-patch hypotheses. This is actually evidence **against** invisibility.

In particular, if actual source packets can approximate a formal source that gives

\[
E_{\eta\eta}\neq0,
\]

then the fixed-sector projection is not identically blind to actual zeta-shaped source data.

This does not prove visibility for every core off-line packet, but it blocks a broad claim that zeta packet symmetry always annihilates fixed-sector odd data.

---

# 8. Attempted open-family invisibility model

A true countermodel would require an open family with

\[
\beta\ne\frac12,
\]

and

\[
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}(\beta,\gamma,m,s)=0
\quad
\forall r\le R_{\Theta_k}
\]

for all admissible nearby \((m,s)\).

I tried the following mechanisms:

1. critical-line symmetry;
2. midpoint evenness;
3. endpoint/sample cancellation;
4. anti-fixed projection;
5. core-normalization absorption;
6. functional-equation/conjugation packet cancellation.

None yields an open-family countermodel outside incidence. Each either requires a boundary condition such as

\[
\beta=\frac12,
\]

a special sample/midpoint choice, or an unproved total-projection identity.

Thus I do not have a valid adversarial counterexample.

---

# 9. What should be added to the exceptional set?

The invisibility locus should remain explicit:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}
=
\{C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}=0\}.
}
\]

The enlarged exceptional set becomes

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core,+}
=
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}.
}
\]

The needed non-swallowing theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm RH}
\not\subseteq
\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}.
}
\]

or stronger:

\[
\boxed{
\forall\ \mathcal P(\rho)\in\mathcal R_{\rm RH},
\quad
\exists (m,s)\in\mathcal B_{\rm red}
\text{ with }
C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}(m,s)\neq0.
}
\]

---

# 10. Final adversarial verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Visibility theorem survives adversarial testing, but remains unproved.}
}
\]

I find no explicit nondegenerate off-line packet family with

\[
C_{\rm fd}^{\vec f}\equiv0
\]

outside controlled incidence.

The strongest adversarial risks are:

\[
\boxed{
\text{core-normalization absorption}
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
\text{total fixed-sector projection annihilation}
}
\]

but both require new identities not currently established.

Therefore the next proof step should be constructive, not adversarial:

\[
\boxed{
\text{show one fixed-sector coefficient }
A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}
\text{ is not identically zero on an off-line packet family.}
}
\]

If that succeeds, packet visibility becomes a proper incidence theorem and the reduced-base rank-two route can proceed.

# Agent 5 — Adversarial Actual-Packet Identity Tester

## Verdict

The proposed nonzero \(A_7^{\mathfrak f}\) witness survives the basic adversarial checks **as a local source-family witness**, but it is **not yet certified as an actual reduced-core packet witness**. The remaining vulnerability is not the algebra of \(A_2,A_4,\nu_{\eta\eta}\); that algebra is decisively nonzero for clean off-line parameters. The vulnerability is whether the same \(A_2,A_4\)-driven row is still present after the packet is treated as part of the fixed core rather than as an exterior/source perturbation. The attached prompt identifies this exact risk: the witness must belong to the actual reduced off-line packet family, not merely to a formal/source-perturbative family. 

---

## 1. Attack on the proposed normalization \(q=1,\ q''=0,\ Q=1\)

Using

\[
q=1,\qquad q''=0,\qquad Q=1
\]

is legitimate as a **local algebraic witness** for non-identity of the coefficient map, provided \(q,q''\) are treated as independent local jet parameters in the analytic family.

It is **not yet legitimate** as an actual zeta-core witness unless the draft proves that, for the reduced off-line packet family, one can realize or approximate a good patch with

\[
q\ne0,\qquad q''=0
\]

or at least with

\[
\frac{A_4}{A_2}
\ne
\frac{10q^2}{9}-\frac{5q''}{18q}.
\]

So the normalization is acceptable for proving that the formula is **not algebraically identically zero** in the enlarged jet model. It is not by itself proof that actual zeta packet visibility holds.

---

## 2. Clean witness calculation

Take

\[
\delta=\frac14,
\qquad
\beta=\frac34,
\]

so

\[
a=\frac12-\delta=\frac14,
\qquad
b=\frac12+\delta=\frac34.
\]

At

\[
x_m=0,
\]

the source moments are

\[
A_2
=
-4(a^{-3}+b^{-3})
=
-4\left(64+\frac{64}{27}\right)
=
-\frac{7168}{27},
\]

and

\[
A_4
=
16(a^{-5}+b^{-5})
=
16\left(1024+\frac{1024}{243}\right)
=
\frac{3997696}{243}.
\]

Thus

\[
A_2\ne0,\qquad A_4\ne0.
\]

The locking ratio is

\[
\frac{A_4}{A_2}
=
-\frac{3997696}{64512}
\approx -61.97.
\]

For the clean normalization

\[
q=1,\qquad q''=0,
\]

the order-7 locking value is

\[
\frac{10q^2}{9}-\frac{5q''}{18q}
=
\frac{10}{9}
\approx 1.11.
\]

So the locking equation fails strongly.

Substituting into

\[
\nu
=
\frac{q}{240}A_2A_4
+
\frac{1}{144}
\left(
\frac{q''}{6}
-
\frac{2q^3}{3}
\right)A_2^2
\]

gives

\[
\nu
=
\frac{1}{240}A_2A_4-\frac{1}{216}A_2^2.
\]

Since

\[
A_2<0,\qquad A_4>0,
\]

both terms are negative. Therefore

\[
\boxed{
\nu\ne0.
}
\]

So, algebraically, the proposed order-7 witness is valid.

---

## 3. Symmetry-packet attack

The paired source atom

\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)
=
\frac{1-\beta}{(1-\beta)^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}
+
\frac{\beta}{\beta^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}
\]

already incorporates the \(\beta\leftrightarrow 1-\beta\) functional-equation pairing in the real phase contribution. It is even in

\[
\delta=\beta-\frac12.
\]

Thus off-lineness appears through

\[
\delta^2,\delta^4,\ldots,
\]

not through a linear \(\delta\)-term. This does **not** cancel the witness. It only means the first nonzero visibility mechanism is even in the off-line displacement.

At

\[
\delta=\frac14,
\]

the paired atom gives nonzero \(A_2,A_4\), so functional-equation pairing does not force invisibility.

Conjugation symmetry also does not visibly cancel the row, because the phase-derivative contribution is already a real even packet contribution at the ordinate level. To produce cancellation, one would need an additional identity showing the conjugate packet contributes the exact negative fixed-sector vector. No such identity is presently in the draft.

---

## 4. Endpoint-centered parity attack

At

\[
x_m=0,
\]

the raw packet function is even in \(u=t-m\). Hence raw odd derivatives vanish.

This does **not** invalidate the witness. The order-7 Bottleneck-C row is not fed by raw odd source derivatives alone. It uses even source moments such as

\[
A_2,\qquad A_4,
\]

which enter the finite-\(s\), phase-gap, whitening, and mixed-block algebra to produce an odd fixed-sector row.

Therefore midpoint evenness is not a counterexample. It only shows that a naive “raw odd derivative” detector would fail. The rank-two fixed-sector detector is subtler.

---

## 5. Attack on \(wI+uS\)

The fixed-sector row is

\[
E_{\eta\eta}
=
-\frac{\nu_{\eta\eta}}{24\pi q^3}(wI+uS).
\]

The clean witness proves only

\[
\nu_{\eta\eta}\ne0.
\]

One must also require

\[
wI+uS\ne0.
\]

This should be a standard good-patch nondegeneracy condition. If

\[
w=u=0,
\]

then the fixed-sector row vanishes regardless of \(\nu\), but that would be a separate degeneracy of the whitening/right-frame slot and should belong to the geometric exceptional set.

So the witness is valid only on patches satisfying

\[
\boxed{
wI+uS\ne0.
}
\]

This is not a serious attack; it is an explicit hypothesis to record.

---

## 6. Fixed-sector projection / whitening attack

Could whitening or fixed-sector projection kill the computed row?

The row is already written in fixed-sector form:

\[
E_{\eta\eta}
=
-\frac{\nu_{\eta\eta}}{24\pi q^3}(wI+uS)
\in \mathfrak f_h.
\]

Thus, after the row has been derived, applying

\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}
\]

does not annihilate it unless

\[
wI+uS=0
\]

or the fixed-sector frame degenerates.

Therefore the projection-annihilation attack fails **for this row formula**.

The remaining caveat is whether this row formula applies to the actual reduced core packet, not merely to the source-perturbative packet. That is a realization/core-absorption issue, not a projection issue.

---

## 7. Core absorption attack — the serious remaining risk

The strongest attack is this:

When the off-line packet is included in

\[
q_{\core},
\]

the packet’s \(A_2,A_4\) moments may be absorbed into the baseline/core model rather than appearing as source perturbation moments. Then the order-7 source-row formula might not directly apply.

In symbols, the witness uses a formula of the form

\[
\nu_{\eta\eta}
=
\frac{q}{240}A_2A_4
+
\frac{1}{144}
\left(
\frac{q''}{6}
-
\frac{2q^3}{3}
\right)A_2^2.
\]

But if the packet is part of the fixed core, the actual coefficient

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}
\]

may be computed from the core-normalized block, not from the same \(A_2,A_4\) source moments.

Therefore the witness is not fully certified until we prove one of the following:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The reduced-core coefficient }A_7^{\mathfrak f}
\text{ contains the same }A_2,A_4\text{ row.}
}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{
\text{Core absorption contributes only controlled baseline terms and cannot cancel the row identically.}
}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{
\text{Even after core normalization, some finite coefficient }A_{2r+1}^{\mathfrak f}\ne0.
}
\]

This is the central remaining gap.

---

## 8. Actual-packet versus source-perturbative family

The proposed witness is definitely in the **source-perturbative analytic family**.

It is in the **actual reduced-core packet family** only if the draft’s construction identifies the packet’s local jet contribution to \(A_7^{\mathfrak f}\) with the same finite-\(s\) row that produced \(E_{\eta\eta}\).

At present, I would phrase the status as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The witness proves non-identity in the local source-row model.}
}
\]

but not yet:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The witness proves non-identity in the actual reduced-core packet coefficient map.}
}
\]

To close the gap, one needs the explicit coefficient formula for

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}
\]

computed from the actual reduced core containing

\[
\mathcal P(\rho).
\]

---

## 9. Can I construct an actual-packet identity \(A_7^{\mathfrak f}\equiv0\)?

No.

I do not find a structural identity forcing

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}\equiv0
\]

for all off-line packets. The following attacks fail:

1. **Functional-equation symmetry:** gives evenness in \(\delta\), not zero.
2. **Conjugation symmetry:** no evident sign cancellation.
3. **Centered midpoint parity:** kills raw odd derivatives, not even-jet-driven fixed-sector rows.
4. **Fixed-sector projection:** cannot kill a row already of the form \(wI+uS\) unless the frame degenerates.
5. **Septic locking:** imposes one equation, not an identity on the whole off-line packet family.

Thus I have no successful countermodel.

---

## 10. Final adversarial clearance

The proposed order-7 witness is cleared under the following assumptions:

\[
\boxed{
\text{A1. The actual reduced-packet }A_7^{\mathfrak f}
\text{ contains the same }A_2,A_4\text{ fixed-sector row as the source model.}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{A2. }q=1,\ q''=0,\ Q=1
\text{ is a legitimate local normalization or can be replaced by an actual good-patch value off the locking hypersurface.}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{A3. }wI+uS\ne0.
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{A4. Full symmetry-packet closure does not add an opposite cancelling contribution.}
}
\]

Under these assumptions, the explicit choice

\[
\delta=\frac14,\qquad x_m=0,\qquad q=1,\qquad q''=0,\qquad Q=1
\]

gives

\[
A_2=-\frac{7168}{27},
\]

\[
A_4=\frac{3997696}{243},
\]

\[
\frac{A_4}{A_2}\ne\frac{10}{9},
\]

and hence

\[
\boxed{
\nu_{\eta\eta}\ne0.
}
\]

So the witness is algebraically nonzero and adversarially robust **except** for the actual-core-realization gap.

---

# Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{No adversarial actual-packet identity has been found.}
}
\]

The nonzero witness is credible but still conditional.

The one missing formula is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Compute }A_7^{\mathfrak f}
\text{ for the actual reduced core containing }\mathcal P(\rho),
}
\]

and verify that the \(A_2,A_4\)-driven row survives core normalization.

If that formula agrees with the source-row witness, then

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core}
\]

is a proper analytic incidence set, and the rank-two route can proceed to the connection theorem. If core normalization cancels it identically, then \(A_9^{\mathfrak f}\) or higher must be computed.

# Agent 5 — Actual Reduced-Core \(A_9^{\mathfrak f}\) Fallback Agent

## Verdict

If the actual reduced-core \(A_7^{\mathfrak f}\) row is cancelled or absorbed, \(A_9^{\mathfrak f}\) is the correct next visibility target. The off-line packet has a nonzero sixth jet

\[
A_6=-64(a^{-7}+b^{-7}),
\qquad
a=\frac12-\delta,\quad b=\frac12+\delta,
\]

so there is new finite jet data available at order 9. However, I cannot certify \(A_9^{\mathfrak f}\neq0\) without the actual reduced-core order-9 row formula. The needed theorem is now very specific: derive whether \(A_9^{\mathfrak f}\) contains a nonzero \(A_6\)-channel after core normalization and fixed-sector projection. The attached instruction identifies exactly this fallback task. 

---

# 1. Available packet jets

At the centered packet point

\[
x_m=2m-\gamma=0,
\]

with

\[
a=\frac12-\delta,\qquad b=\frac12+\delta,
\]

the packet moments are:

\[
A_2=-4(a^{-3}+b^{-3}),
\]

\[
A_4=16(a^{-5}+b^{-5}),
\]

\[
\boxed{
A_6=-64(a^{-7}+b^{-7}).
}
\]

For every

\[
0<|\delta|<\frac12,
\]

we have

\[
a,b>0,
\]

so

\[
a^{-7}+b^{-7}>0,
\]

and therefore

\[
\boxed{
A_6\neq0.
}
\]

For the clean witness

\[
\delta=\frac14,
\qquad
a=\frac14,\qquad
b=\frac34,
\]

this gives

\[
A_6
=
-64\left(4^7+\left(\frac43\right)^7\right)
=
-\frac{2294284288}{2187}
\neq0.
\]

So the packet has nonzero sixth-order source/core data available for order 9.

---

# 2. Why \(A_9^{\mathfrak f}\) is the right fallback

The order-7 coefficient can vanish on the septic locking hypersurface

\[
F_7(A_2,A_4,q,q'')=0,
\]

equivalently

\[
\frac{A_4}{A_2}
=
\frac{10q^2}{9}
-
\frac{5q''}{18q}.
\]

This is one analytic equation involving

\[
A_2,\quad A_4,\quad q,\quad q''.
\]

Order 9 should be the first place where the next independent packet jet

\[
A_6
\]

can enter. A typical order-9 fixed-sector row would be expected to involve channels such as

\[
qA_2A_6,
\qquad
qA_4^2,
\qquad
q''A_2A_4,
\qquad
q^{(4)}A_2^2,
\qquad
q^3A_2A_4,
\qquad
q^5A_2^2,
\]

or comparable terms depending on the exact finite-\(s\) expansion.

The key point is:

\[
\boxed{
F_7=0
\text{ constrains }A_4/A_2,
\text{ but it does not by itself constrain }A_6.
}
\]

Thus, unless the actual reduced-core algebra imposes an additional structural identity tying

\[
A_6
\]

to

\[
A_2,A_4,q,q'',q^{(4)},
\]

order 9 should generically restore visibility.

---

# 3. Independence check at the packet level

The centered packet jets satisfy

\[
A_2=-4(a^{-3}+b^{-3}),
\]

\[
A_4=16(a^{-5}+b^{-5}),
\]

\[
A_6=-64(a^{-7}+b^{-7}).
\]

Let

\[
r_n:=a^{-n}+b^{-n}.
\]

Then

\[
A_2=-4r_3,\qquad A_4=16r_5,\qquad A_6=-64r_7.
\]

The septic locking equation constrains only the ratio

\[
\frac{r_5}{r_3}.
\]

It does not force a fixed value of

\[
r_7.
\]

For example, with

\[
a=\frac14,\quad b=\frac34,
\]

we get

\[
r_3=64+\frac{64}{27},
\]

\[
r_5=1024+\frac{1024}{243},
\]

\[
r_7=16384+\frac{16384}{2187}.
\]

These are not related by a universal linear identity of the form

\[
r_7=\alpha r_5+\beta r_3
\]

with constants independent of \(a,b\). They belong to the moment sequence of two distinct positive scales \(a,b\). Thus \(A_6\) is genuinely new packet data.

This supports the fallback claim:

\[
\boxed{
A_7^{\mathfrak f}=0
\text{ should be codimension one, and }
A_7^{\mathfrak f}=A_9^{\mathfrak f}=0
\text{ should be codimension at least two, absent structural cancellation.}
}
\]

---

# 4. What must be shown in the actual reduced-core row

The required reduced-core order-9 formula should have the form

\[
A_9^{\mathfrak f}
=
\mathcal R_9(A_2,A_4,A_6,q,q'',q^{(4)},\ldots)\,I
+
\mathcal S_9(A_2,A_4,A_6,q,q'',q^{(4)},\ldots)\,S.
\]

Visibility at order 9 follows if at least one of

\[
\mathcal R_9,\qquad \mathcal S_9
\]

contains an \(A_6\)-channel with nonzero coefficient after imposing

\[
F_7=0.
\]

The decisive test is:

\[
\boxed{
\left.
\frac{\partial A_9^{\mathfrak f}}{\partial A_6}
\right|_{F_7=0}
\neq0
}
\]

as a fixed-sector vector coefficient.

If this derivative is nonzero, then the packet’s nonzero

\[
A_6
\]

gives an order-9 witness outside a further analytic incidence set.

If the derivative vanishes identically, then order 9 does not help and one must inspect order 11.

---

# 5. Possible cancellation scenarios

There are three possible outcomes.

## Case A — \(A_9^{\mathfrak f}\) has a nonzero \(A_6\)-channel

Then visibility survives order-7 locking.

The invisibility locus becomes

\[
\{A_7^{\mathfrak f}=0,\ A_9^{\mathfrak f}=0\},
\]

a proper higher-codimension incidence set unless another identity forces both.

## Case B — \(A_9^{\mathfrak f}\) contains \(A_6\), but core normalization cancels it

Then one must identify the exact cancellation term. This would be a serious structural fact about the reduced-core normalization.

The next target would be order 11 and the \(A_8\) or \(A_{10}\) packet jets, depending on parity conventions.

## Case C — \(A_9^{\mathfrak f}\) has no \(A_6\)-channel

Then order 9 is not the correct fallback. The first order at which the sixth packet jet enters must be identified from the finite-\(s\) row algebra.

---

# 6. Conditional theorem

The strongest theorem I can state without the explicit order-9 row is:

**Conditional \(A_9\) fallback theorem.**  
Assume that in the actual reduced-core expansion,

\[
A_9^{\mathfrak f}
\]

contains a nonzero fixed-sector channel proportional to

\[
A_2A_6
\quad\text{or}\quad
A_6
\]

with coefficient nonzero on a good patch. Then an off-line packet satisfying

\[
0<|\delta|<\frac12
\]

and the reduced-good-patch assumptions has

\[
A_9^{\mathfrak f}\not\equiv0
\]

on the order-7 locking hypersurface, outside a proper analytic incidence set.

Reason: \(A_6\neq0\), and \(F_7=0\) does not impose \(A_6=0\) or a universal cancellation relation involving \(A_6\).

---

# 7. Missing formula

The exact missing object is:

\[
\boxed{
A_9^{\mathfrak f}
=
[s^9]\pi_{\mathfrak f}
\widehat\Omega_{\zeta}^{\corr,\core}(s;m)
}
\]

in the actual reduced-core convention.

More specifically, we need the coefficient of

\[
A_6
\]

inside

\[
A_9^{\mathfrak f}.
\]

Until this is derived, one cannot certify the \(A_9\) fallback as a theorem.

---

# Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
A_6\neq0
\text{ for every nondegenerate centered off-line packet.}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
A_7^{\mathfrak f}=0
\text{ imposes only one analytic locking equation and does not control }A_6.
}
\]

Therefore \(A_9^{\mathfrak f}\) is the correct fallback and should generically restore visibility.

But:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The actual reduced-core }A_9^{\mathfrak f}\text{ row formula is still missing.}
}
\]

The next concrete computation is to derive the \(A_6\)-channel in

\[
A_9^{\mathfrak f}.
\]

If it is nonzero, packet visibility is essentially reduced to a proper analytic incidence theorem. If it is zero or cancels, the fallback shifts to the next odd coefficient.
